WAITING FOR THE
FEDERAL TROOPS
Wardner Citizens Dread
Further Violence.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WARDNER, Idaho, May I.â There
were no disturbances in the mining dis
trict to-day. Eleven men were seen
from Wardner going over the moun
tains, each with a rifle over his shoul
der, but their identity or destination
was not known. A considerable num
ber of non-union men left on the train
to-day, also a few strikers, Citizens
are in dread of further outrages from
now until the arrival of troops.
, State Auditor Bartlett Sinclair ar
rived here to-day as a representative
of Governor Stuenenburg. He is search- j
ing : for evidence against the dyna- ;
miters, but cannot make much headway
before the arrival of troops. James !
Ch.yne, wounded by dynamiters on
Saturday, is not expected to live until
morning:
To-day the body of Jack Smith, who
was killed by his fellow-rioters, was
brought down from the Miners' Union
Hall in Burke and is at Wallace in j
charge of an undertaking flrm named
by Coroner France. The remains will
be brought here in time for the in
quest. This will be held at 2 o'clock
Saturday afternoon. Unless the sol
diers have arrived in the meantime the
inquest may be postponed.
A telephone message to-day from
Manager Burkridge of the Bunker Hill
and Sullivan mine directed that the old
force stay together here In expectation
of the revival of work. Excavation of
the ruins of the mill to-day disclosed
the fact that the vault and safe had
been successfully blown open by the
rioters before the building itself was
destroyed. Only a few smoldering ashes
were found within the safe. The loss
to the company from the destruction of
hundreds of valuable documents will
be immense. _
MARTIAL LAW WILL
SOON BE DECLARED
SPOKANE, Wash., May I.â Martial law
will be declared in the Coeur d'Alenes.
Bartlett Sinclair, the State Auditor, who
is at Wardner as the personal representa
tive of the Governor, said there to-night
that the situation was Infinitely worse
than he had realized, and that martial
law is imperatively necessary. He ap
proves the demand for 500 troops made
by Governor Steunenberg and wired the
Governor to-night to urge its being
granted. So far as known no troops have
yet been ordered. The one company of
regulars at Spokane Is still under waiting
orders to move. _ _,_
Some of the non-union miners and Man
ager Burbidge have returned to the Bun
ker Hill and Sullivan. The men are clear
ing away the ruins. The destruction was
complete. A largo number of papers of
Immense value to the property were de
stroyed, including documents of the ut
most weight in litigation now pending be
tween the Bunker Hill and Sullivan and
Last Chance.
COLORED REGULARS
ORDERED TO MOVE
SPOKANE, May Tho company of
colored regulars stationed at the Spokane
army post received orders late to-night
direct from General Merrlam to leave on
the morning train for Wardner, Idaho.
The company at Fort Walla Walla has
received similar orders.
Bartlet Sinclair, Auditor of Idaho, in an
interview with the Spokesman-Review
correspondent at Wardner to-night, said:
"The conditions here are far worse than
I could possibly imagine. The prosecut
ing attorney and Sheriff have been
shamefully remiss in their duty. I told
them so to-day. They probably will be
Impeached. Existing conditions here
must cease. The State authorities, aided
by Federal troops, will see to it."

CROOKED WORK
IN DREYFUS CASE
Revelations of Colonel
Paty dv Clam.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PARIS, May 2.â The Figaro, continu
ing its publica..on of testimony offered
by the Court of Cassation in the Drey-'
fus inquiry, prints to-day the deposi
tion of Lieutenant Colonel Paty dv
Clam, who on April 29 deposed that be
ing invited to reply frankly to the
statements of Captain Cuignet, he had
obeyed as a soldier, without asking
written orders from his superior, con
tenting himself with oral ones.
Referring to his silence in the face of
accusations and to his being placed on
the retired list, the witness asserted'
that he was "the most f disciplined of
soldiers" and had merited the fate
meted, out to him. He. demanded to be
confronted with Captain Cuignet and
the other accusers. -; " '
In replying to "numerous questions he
said that when General Ie Mouton Bois
deffre, chief of the French general
staff in 1894, wanted him to act as
police officer in the Dreyfus affair, he
asked to be excused and recommended
Lieutenant Colonel Picquart for the
task. General Bolsdeffre refused to
accept this suggestion.
Throughout the inquiry, the witness
affirmed, he had only acted on the ad
vice of the experts in handwriting, MM.
Bertillon and Chocherfort. M. Bertil
lon advised him to dictate lines to !
Dreyfus and therefore accompanied
him.
The report was presented in October,
1894, It did not draw the inference of
the guilt of Dreyfus, but merely stated
certain facts. In December, 1894, Colonel
Sandherr sent him (Paty dv Clam) a
dossier of secret documents to report
upon, and the report which was the
result of his collaboration with
Colonel Sandherr went to General .Mur
der, then Minister of War.
Coming down to the events of 1896,
Colonel Paty dv Clam said he was
ignorant of the doings of the intelli
gence bureau during this period, but
he indicated M. Decrion as being at the
head of a veritable manufactory of
forgeries for Lieutenant Colonel Henry.
(The Figaro remarks that M. Decrion's
evidence has been forgotten or sup
pressed in the dossier of the inquiry.)
Colonel Paty dv Clam said General
Gonze told him in 1597 that he had
minutely investigated the affair and
had proved Esterhazy's innocence of
treason. Again in 1898 General Gonze
told him it was imperative to save
Esterhazy at any price' in order to pre
vent his committal of the Irreparable
acts of suicide or flight and also to
avoid diplomatic difficulties, as war,
owing to the lack of preparations,
would then be disastrous.
It was decided, the witness contin
ued, to save Esterhazy and MM. Grib
bel and Pauffln de St. Morel and the
witness were intrusted with the task.
Then for th*'- first time the witness
acted, he declared, with Esterhazy,
whom he had only seen once in Al
geria eighteen years, previously. He
accepted the responsibility for what he
had done, but declared that he only
acted on orders. He admitted giving
Esterhazy a draft of the articles signed
"Dixi," and also drafts of letters ad
dressed to the President of the republic,
the sending of which was authorized
and covered by General Boisdeffre after
a conference with General Gonze.
In the course of the examination
Colonel Paty dv Clam denied that
he had made any communications to
the press such as Captain Cuignet had
accused him of doing, adding that this
kind of work was done by Henry and
Pauffln de St. Morel.
Paty dv Clam, in the course of his
evidence, said that when M. Cavaignac
became Minister of War he (Paty dv
Clam) wished to warn him of Lien
tenant Colonel Henry's forgery, but the
Minister refused to see him until after
an interpellation had been made.
When he told the Minister that he
had doubts concerning certain docu
ments M. Cavaignac replied: "Mind
your own business."
This statement, the Figaro says, pro
duced a sensation in the court and
Paty dv Clam became frightened and
asked to have it suppressed.
The witness further said that Lieu
tenant Colonel Henry alluded to Gen
eral Mercier when he wrote to his wife
"you know in whose interest I -have
acted."
Replying to a direct quesction
Paty dv Clam said: "General de Bois
deffre promised to cover me; now he
sacrifices me."
GUNBOAT NASHVILLE
RUNS UP TO MEMPHIS
Tennesseeans Give a Great Ovation to

and Crew.
MEMPHIS, Term., May I.â The United
States .gunboat Nashville entered Mem

the officers of the ship at the Peabody
Hotel.

BONANZA STRIKE
ON WRIGHT CREEK
New Gold Field Opened
to Klondikers.
Special Dispatch' to The Call.
VICTORIA, B. C.. May The
steamer Danube reached port this
afternoon after a fast trip from Lynn
canal ports. She made the down trip
in eighty-five hours. News was
brought by. passengers who arrived by
the Danube of a fabulously rich find
which is reported at Atlin City to, have
been made on Wright Creek. One of
the claim owners on that creek reports
having taken out over $600 in a day.
When news of the flnd was reported at
Atlin City it caused immense excite
ment and a big exodus of miners im
mediately occurred. The miners rushed
off pellmell to get claims on the rich
ground.
Among the Danube's passengers was
H. J. Cameron, one of the rich men of
the Klondike. He owns several bench
claims on Gold Run and has properties
on other Dawson creeks. With Fred
Hall, who accompanied him out, he
brought $35,000 in gold dust and' drafts.
They left Dawson on April 13. When
they left, they say, miners were already
beginning to arrive at the capita} with
gold washed from their dumps on the
creeks. The gold output, according to
the estimates of the majority, will be
over $30,000,000.
The trail is now all broken up. It
is no longer fit for travel. Fifty Mile
and Thirty Mile are both open. Re
ports were current at some of the road
houses of the drowning of miners by
falling through the ice, but none of the
reports could be verified.
Just before the Danube left Skaguay
a citizen was robbed of $400. Several
other robberies have occurred of late
and Skaguayans are afraid the "Soapy"

CITIZEN MELANDER
RELEASED BY SPANIARDS
NEW YORK, May I.â Washington
special to the Herald says: Secretary
Hay was formally notified to-day of the
release by the Spanish garrison at
Ponape, Caroline Islands, of a naturalized
American citizen named Melander, held
as a prisoner of war since last summer.
Mr. Melander is a merchant trading in
the Carolines, and represents a San Fran
cisco firm there. He avowed his Ameri
can citizenship soon after the war began,
and expressed sympathy for the Ameri
can Government. He was arrested and
held as a prisoner of war. News travels
slowly from the Carolines, and it was
sometime before the San Francisco firm
heard of the imprisonment of its agent.
It notified the State Department, and this
Government requested the British Gov
ernment to use its good offices to secure
Meander's release. The British Embas
sador to Spain presented the case to the
Spanish Government, which a short time
ago sent Instructions to the Carolines di- ,
recting the release of the American. I

FORTY BUILDINGS
RAZED BY FLAMES
Sebastopol's Chinatown
Destroyed.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA ROSA', May I.â Sebastopol
was visited by a disastrous conflagra
tion this mrning. A strong wind
blowing from the north was all that
saved the entire place from destruction.
More than forty buildings were burned.
The fire started in a small frame
building in the center of Chinatown at
8:30 o'clock. For more than an hour it
raged fiercely and it died out only when
it had destroyed everything in its path.
Sebastopol has no fire department
and little could be done to check the
spread of the flames. Not much dam
age was done outside the Chinese quar
ter, but there is not enough of that left
to make even a showing on the map.
Except in the building where the fire
originated the Celestials saved most of
their personal effects. It is reported
that in one house the flames destroyed
a trunk containing a large sum of
money. A number of Chinese are now
guarding the spot where the melted
gold is said to be.
As a result of the conflagration
Sebastopol's Chinese to-night are with
out a place to la./ their heads. -No. esti
mate of the. loss has yet been! ' jrnad<?,
but it is large and falls principally
upon the heirs of . the - late Aaron
Barnes. ,' â¢ : ; . >: \ ' - y y \,
SECRETARY ALGER
AFTER SENATORSHIP
' NEW YORK, Many I.â -Washington
special to the Herald says: Secretary Al
ger's qualifled announcement of his can
didacy for election to the Senate from
Michigan, provided he does not have to
scramble for the oflice, is regarded as the
beginning of his campaign to succeed Sen
ator McMillan, whose term will expire on
March 3, 1901. Considerable comment was
caused by his statement that he had Sen
ator McMillan's word that he did not ex
pect to be a candidate and that the Sen
ator had promised to aid him all he could.
This caused considerable surprise among
the personal and political friends of Sen
ator McMillan, for they fully understand
that he expects to be a candidate for re
election. One of the Senator's friends
suggested to me to-day that Secretary
! Alger's purpose in talking was to try to
draw from Senator McMillan a definite
j declaration as to whether he desired an
other term in the Senate. Should Secre
: tary Alger succeed in having himself se
lected to the Senate he would regard it as
a vindication of his course at tl_Â» head of
' the War Department. If Senator McMil
i lan determines to be a candidate' for re
| election, however. it is not believed here
that Secretary Alger would be able to de-

BERKELEY BOYS
PERFORM IK A
TWO-RING .ROW
Clever Circus by the
Athletes.
EDDIE DIXON MAKES A HIT
HIS WORK AS LEADER OF THE
CLOWNS IS CLEVER.
The Famed Oval a Mass of Bril
liant Life and Color on the Oc
casion of the First College
Performance.
A circus without sawdust, and still a !
success! The deficiency was made up by ;
pink lemonade, peanuts, leapers and a !
wealth of clowns at Berkeley Oval yes
terday. The occasion was the first col- '
lege circus promoted by the students of |
the University of California. It was a
marked success throughout. The clowns I
were genuinely funny, the acrobats of the
college, with here and there a professional I
performer, made a programme of merit
throughout. The amphitheater was
thronged, a four-ln-hand drag and a num- ;
ber of fashionable equipages adding to j
the picturesqueness of the scene.
It was all done after the manner of '
the only P. T. Barnum of "the greatest j
show on earth." from the grand entree ;
of all the performers to the side show and
concert. There was the customary street i
k parade, which attracted every small boy
within a radius of miles. Despite the
vigilance of the police, these same small
hoys saw all the show, although the box
office receipts do not indicate their pres
ence. The pageant presented a striking
appearance In the grand entry. First
came a number of mounted Indians in
full war paint and feathers, headed by
the redoubtable Easy, Easy Kaarsburg.
Once well on the cinder path they gal- j
loped around on their ponies, discharging
volley after volley from their revolvers. '
After them followed the college band, led J
by Howard Squires as a drum major.
Then came the performers, the acro
bats, the clowns, Amazons, Uncle Sam j
and the Yellow Kid. Tom Carroll, the
herculean hammer-thrower, clothed in a j
blonde wig, ballet girl's skirts and blue !
and gold striped stockings, passed as j
Little Eva, and was drawn in a chariot.
Carl Parker, dressed as a beau, made a |
decided hit, In company with Mendenhall, ;
his keeper, who developed an accent with
a rich Italian flavor.
The clowns included Eddie Dixon,
Womble, McEwen, Cohen, Duden and'
Zederman. Dixon carried off premier
honors, both as a clever gymnast and
champion funmaker. He was ably sec- I
onded by Womble. The only "Monk"
Chesebrough was an impressive ring- |
master, clothed in top boots, corduroy
riding breeches, a swallow-tail coat and
silk hat.
After two circuits of the cinder path tho
I* apers and tumblers came out and gave
a clever exhibition on the springboard.
A professional tight rope walker gave a
clever performance and was followed by
i. contortionist who twisted himself nt :
Tnany shapes while performing on a flying '
trapeze. A quartet of the clownsâ
Tuny, Argall and Plcotâ sang a number ;
of ragtime ditties.
Tom Carroll, Plaw nnd Al Lean toyed j
with monster dumbbells. As a finish to ' '
their work a heavy stone was placed on
Carroll's chest and Droken with a blow
from a heavy hammer wielded by Plaw.
They were succeeded by a professional
juggler, who was expert in handling
knives, brass balls and other parapher
nalia. An Amazon march was a feature
of the programme. The Amazons were
husky members of the baseball and foot
ball teams. They all wore blonde wigs
and the abbreviated costume of ballet
dancers. They were armed with rifles
and were an awkward squad.
There was boxing and wrestling by
Acme and Reliance Club men. The box
ing between Woodrum and Ford developed
into a hard-hitting match, and after some
good red blood had been spilled in the j
mimic warfare, the bout was stopped.
The clowns were never quiet throughout
the afternoon. They boxed and wrestled,
played leap frog and rough house, per
formed on tbe bar and rode dummy
horses true to life. They also gave a
clever imitation of football practice. The
method of falling on a ball, as taught by
"Gamey" Cochran and the method of the
Stanford men were both shown with the i
aid of an inflated bladder. The dainty!
method of the Stanford man greatly ;
amused the spectators. . j
The principal sideshow was supposed to
contain the famous Stanford ax. The
tent was guarded by a squad of the uni
versity cadets armed with rifles and bayo
nets. " The real ax was not on view. A
negro, whom the spieler called a Filipino.
had his head poked through a big con
vas and the strong-arm boys took turn
shooting balls at his head.
The affair was a marked success, add
ing over $MX) to the funds of the univer
sity athletic association. The commit
tee of arrangements was made up of I.
J. Muma, chairman; J. C. Allen, proper
ties and finance; M. H. Schwartz, side
shows; J. B. Southard, director of pro
Athletes Who Played Star Parts at University of California Circus.

Scalded by Escaping Steam.
ANGELS CAMP, May I.â John Inlinger,
a German, died at Utica Hospital this
afternoon from injuries received at the
Angels Brewery on Saturday afternoon.
The unfortunate brewer was about to
open the blow-off valve on the boiler,
when a steam pipe burst, the steam lit
erally cooking him alive. He lingered
until to-day. He was 20 years of age and
unmarried.

HARRIS' MEN
MAKE A SHOW
OF OAKLAND
Frisco Captures a
Lopsided Game.
TAKE AN UNFAIR ADVANTAGE
DAZZLE THEIR OPPONENTS WITH
GAUDY GROUNDS.
Two Pitchers Crippled in a Vain
Effort to Put a Check to Grow
ing Ratting Averages
of the Locals.
Hank Harris and his hired men cele
brated Dewey day by winning about as
lopsided a game from Oakland as has
been played at Recreation Park this sea
son. Hank and his assistants got up
early in the morning, and with the aid
of large quantities of vari-colored kal
somine decorated the diamond until it
looked like a three-sneet poster of a
circus. The foul lines were marked out
In alternate sections of red, white and
blue, the bases were similarly decorated
and so was the surrounding landscape.
Just in front of the plate .the name
"Dewey" in . large, colored letters gave
the spectators an idea of what it all
meant.
The grounds looked gorgeous, but it
was an underhanded advantage to take of
the cripples, for whenever an Oaklander
started for first he Imagined he was
climbing a barber pole.
Frisco batted out the game right
enough, but Oakland's bunglesâ
ticularly those of Lee Hammondâ were
what made the score so top heavy. Shea
started in to wrap dp the packages for
the Athenians, but in the sixth inning,
after the game was nopelessly lost, he
was taken out of the box and Mosklman
substitute
Six hits were made off Shea's delivery,
four of which were bunched in the fourth.
These, with errors, let in four runs. In
the fifth only one hit was made off him
and that had no bearing on the result, but
Shea went up in the air, gave three bases
on balls, which, coupled with errors,
netted three more tallies.
Moskiman got along very nicely until
the last Inning, when he, too, was sub
jected to a cannonading, three singles and
a double, with accompanying bungles,
giving the home team another- quartet of
sign marks. ;; â¢ .â¢ -.
Yesterday's game demonstrated that
Harris has a brace of twirlers that can
hold their own, or a little better, with
any in the league. Fitzpatrick started in
to pitch, but in the last half of the third
inning O'Neii became involved in a dis
pute with Umpire Levy that resulted in
his being set down, and "Fitz" had to go
into the garden, while Iberg finished the
game. Five was the sum total of
Oakland's hits off Iberg, but one of them|j
was a beautiful triple by Donovan. It
was that shot, followed by a single by
Billy Hammond, that saved Oakland from
the horrors of a shut-out.
Captain Tip's argument with Umpire
Rube was one of the incidents of the
game. O'Neii was at bat and Levy called a
strike on him that was not to Tip's liking.
Refore he had an opportunity to register
a kick another ball sailed up to the plate
and he touched off a single. While rest
ing at first Tip made some unkind re
marks about cross-eyed umpires that lac- !
crated Rube's feelings, so he walked
over to the bag to voice his protest i
against such satirical language. O'Neii
advised Levy to go back to his job and
try and do better in the future. That was
too. too much, so Rube sent Tip to the
bench then and there, Pyne being sent
out to run the bases for his captain.
Stub exchanges of amenities merely
add flavor to the game, however.
Hank Introduced his new catcher,
Swindells, to the home patrons, and the
youngster made a most favorable impres
sion. He handles himself like a veteran,
seems to keep his head at all times. is a
strong, accurate thrower to bases and a
good sticker. Here's to a long and suc
cessful baseball career for the young
man. After O'Neii was retired Pyne i
came in and played second, and was an
agreeable surprise as an inlielder to his
friends. He played third at San Jose
Saturday, Riley being ill, and Harris says
there is not a faster lnflelder ln the
league. It might not be a bad idea for
Harris to change Pyne and Krug, for the
latter is a lightning outfielder, but liable
to go Into the air at critical times when
playing an infield position. - .":
Muller distinguished himself in the sec
ond inning by retiring the side in order.
Three Oakland men in succession drove
out long and difficult flies, all of which
Muller pulled down. The score:

GREAT REINFORCEMENT
FOR THE STATE MINERS
SOUTHERN CALIFORNIA IS NOW
IN LINE.
Officers of the California Miners' As
sociation Form an Allied Branch
With Steve White for
President.
Yesterday's returning owl train from
Los Angeles brought back to the city a
jolly party of representative members of
the California Miners' Association who
accomplished an important feat at Los
Angeles last Saturday eveningâ lmportant
as far as the mining industry of the State
is concerned.
They succeeded in organizing a South
ern California branch .of the California
Miners' Association, thereby strengthen
ing greatly that already strong organi
sation that has been able to secure much
important national and State legislation
in the interest of the mining industry.
Shortly after the last annual convention
of the State association, at which E. H.
Benjamin was elected secretary, Mr. Ben
jamin began a general effort by corre
spondence and otherwise to enlarge the
membership by organizing mining coun
ties that were not among the fourteen
then affiliated with the association. None
of the southern counties which have re
cently come forward into prominence in
the mining world had ever been organ
ized or been part of the association. Mr.
Benjamin wrote many letters to southern
mining men and some leading ones re
sponded in an energetic way. Prominent
among them was O. Breese. one of the
editors of the Mining and Metallurgical
Journal of Los Angeles. , "
Two or three months of agitation down
there resulted in a general movement for
organization which soon became charac

terlzed by the idea that Southern Califor
nia should organize by itself independent
ly of the California Miners' Association,
and then ally itself with similar organiza
tions of Arizona and New Mexico. This
particular agitation shows how easily
"Southern California" regards itself as
something apart from the rest of the
State. â 
A general meeting for organization was
arranged for last Saturday evening in the
rooms of the Chamber of Commerce at
Los Angeles. The members of the Cali
fornia Miners' Association who went
were: Secretary E. H. Benjamin, A. H.
Ricketts, who for years has been chair
man of the mineral lands committee and
led in fighting that battle; Attorney Gen
eral Tirey L. Ford, who is chairman of
the committee on a national department
of mines and mining: Senator John F. Da
vis of Amador, chairman of the Senate
Mining Committee, and Charles G. Yale.
President Jacob H. Neff was detained by
sickness, and Vice President Ralston tele
graphed his proxy to ex-Senator Stephen
M. White with the request that he repre
sent him.
The Chamber of Commerce at Los An
geles was packed Saturday night. Secre
tary Benjamin called the meeting to or
der, and Stephen M. White became chair
man. An animated discussion lasted for
hours. The representatives of the Cali
fornia Miners' Association all pleaded for
unity and others supported them.
The upshot was that a resolution or
ganizing a Southern California branch of
the State association was passed withou*
one dissenting vote. Stephen M. White
was made president and Mr. Breese sec
retary. By this action the association
gains greatly in prestige and influence,
and the unity of the mining industry in
California is assured. County organiza
tions will be formed in the seven south
ern counties, which will unite in formi'^
a Southern. California organization, with
headquarters at Los Angeles, but this or
ganization will be part of the California
Miners' Association. It was a triumph
for the mining industry of the State. The
missionaries returned yesterday and
brought Mr. White along.
STOLE A BICYCLE.
George Whipple, alias Smith, alias
Cook, was arrested last night by Detec
tives Ryan and ODea and charged with
burglary. Whipple was employed as i
waiter "in the Hotel Bradbury, and be
coming dissatisfied with his position he
broke Into the storeroom and stole a bi
cycle, the property of Bert Wirthmlere.
a guest at the hostelry. He sold the
wheel for a few dollars and made ar
rangements to leave the city.
Last evening he was found walking
along Market street and taken into cus
tody. It is claimed that Whipple is re
sponsible for numerous thefts committed
at the hotel.

AN IMPREGNABLE ADMIRAL.
THE nomination by the Examiner of Admiral
Dewey, once of Vermont but now the naval
representative of the Union, as the Democratic
candidate for the Presidency in [900 is such colossal
impudence that it indicates hopeless imbecility. The
freaks and hysterics of that absurd representative of
morbid egotism have been tolerated for ears, for the
same reason that the antics of a callow youth, ine
briated with his own importance, ire indulgently per
mitted.
When the Examiner-Journal or the Journal- 1
Examiner offered a reward of $50,000 for the discov
ery of the destroyer of the Maine, American citizen
ship within all parties faintly smiled. When it ap
pointed a congressional delegation to supervise and
expedite the work of the Government commission
the public amusement became more pronounced.
When it sent or pretended to send its "Commission
ers*' to crowned heads and other rulers, civil and
ecclesiastical, there was an international chuckle.
When it announced its "national policy," which has
1 een two or three times transmogrified, there was
an unnecessary but derisive laugh. When it pla
carded its "internal policy. the laugh- became a roar.
All these absurd contortions could be endured.
But. by Jupiter, the attempt to appropriate Admiral
Dewey is too much. To convert any distinguished
Vcrmonter into a fusion Democrat, even within his
native State, would exhaust the combined influence
of the Cuban bondholders and the Asiatic specula
tors. To propose the capture of a great national
htro. who has reached the highest dignity among
men in the service of the Government, under a Re-
publican administration, and who has already rejected
every political overture, is a step in advance of all
the idiotic sublimities of the pas It is tolerably safe
to assume that Admiral Dewey will prefer his cocked
hat and his naval standing to the conglomeration of
free silver and socialistic demand? that constitute the
stock in trade of the Examiner.
The Examiner had better resume it- interrupted
employment of converting W. J. Bryan to impe
rialism.
fIMERICfINS IN NICARAGUA.
RECENT reports from Washington are to the
effect that advices received by our Government
from Nicaragua indicate that the situation there
is extremely discouraging to American interests. It
is being impressed upon our officials, so it is said, that
persons high in the Nicaraguan Government are
pursuing a policy deliberately designed to crush
American business interests, now of considerable
magnitude in that country.
Such reports are not surprising. It was but a short
time ago that information was received here of the
inauguration of a political movement in Nicaragua to
bring about the annexation of the country to the
United States. The leaders of the movement were
said to be President Zelaya. General Estrada, Senor
Guzman and others of hardly less note and influence.
It was added that next year a party with annexation
as the principal plank in its platform would appeal to
the people nd make a strong campaign in its favor.
From these reports it is evident that American in
terests in Nicaragua have now become so important
and American influence so strong as to be the domi
nant factors in the politics of the people. Of one
party, it is alleged, the leaders de-ire to crush our
interests; and of the other, the leaders desire to
make our interests paramount even to the surrender
of Nicaraguan sovereignty. -
The American people arc sufficiently acquainted
with charges and countercharges of political parties
to know how to appraise them at something like their
true worth. A good deal of allowance will, there
fore, be mafic for exaggeration on both sides. The
situation, however, is clearly otic that merits watch
ing. The annexation of Hawaii has in all probability
excited in the minds of some Americans in Nicaragua
a desire, to emulate the example of the Americans in
Honolulu. It i- possible that some of them may be
meddling in Nicaraguan politics for the express pur
pose of bringing abot.t a condition of affairs that will
lead to the development of an annexation sentiment
not only there but in the United States.
We have unquestionably great interests in Nica
ragua which must be carefully guarded. The early
undertaking of the construction of the interocean
canal is now well assured, and when that route is
open the How of commerce across Nicaragua will be
enormous. The advocates of unlimited annexation
will therefore have a strong argument on their side,
and they now seem to be getting ready to spring it on
the country. It therefore behooves the people to
watch the issue well or we may have a Nicaraguan
mnexation scheme confronting us I fore we have de
vised a form of government for Hawaii.
The action of Lieutenant Conger in commanding
that the monkey pets of the soldiers returning on the
transport Grant should be thrown into the sea, be-
cause he was afraid they might spread smallpox, may
be taken as an evidence of the recklessness of life
produced by the war. Is not a loyal monkey
enough of a man and a brother to be vaccinated and
saved to grow up with the country ?
The stars and stripes have been raised over business
houses at Bluefields as an emblem of protection as
well as of freedom. The war with Spain has demon
strated to some of our foreign friends that Uncle Sam
can fight when he wants to aud the mood suits him.
THE BATTLE OF MANILA BAY.
YESTERDAY the achievements of Admiral
Dewey and the force under his command were
â¢ appropriately and grandly celebrated. The
clustered glories with which the valor, the training,
the discipline and the in belligence of our navy in
vested the national flag h;eve been depicted in glow
ing eloquence, both in pros** and in verse. The practi
cal aspen- of the destructh of the Spanish squadron
at Manila Bay now deserve consideration.
The lesson of the battle ivas that the character of
naval engagements has radically changed. The re
sult is now largely determined by the excellence of
the materials and the mechanical skill applied to the
construction of war vessels, by the scientific precision
with which they are handle*! and by -the accurate
use of the heavy armaments that have replaced the
lighter guns of former times. The victory at Manila
was a triumphant expression <*:f American science and
of American training, as well as a demonstration of
the superiority of the blended strains of blood de
veloped by our institutions into the composite type
â  -- â -â .-.
of American citizenship.
Our loss was trifling. The loss of the enemy was
comparatively severe. It is probable that in the naval
battles of the future this disproportion will be sub
stantially maintained. Defeated fleets will be de
stroyed beyond the possibility -of rehabilitation. The
victors ordinarily will be almost unscathed. Engage
ments will be short and decisive. One side or the
other will be speedily vanquished. Drawn battles
will be unknown. Ships will not unite iii the bloody
hand-to-hand struggles of the past. 'Hie ability to
support enormous expenditures will be of almost as
great consequence as the human factors in naval con
tests. The sacrifice of life will be insignificant, and,
while now as ever heroism will find its opportunities
and merit its rewards, the power of money will be as
ascendant in war as it has become in the piping
times of peace. .
The American people and their ancestors have al
ways possessed maritime genius. If is historically
recorded that, in 1340. in a naval battle off the harbor
of Slavs, the English King, Edward 111. with his
forces, slew 30.000 Frenchmen, and captured 200 ves
sels, in one of which alone 400 dead bodies were
found. Our revolutionary struggle had scarcely be
gun before the Dolphin, the Lexington and the Re
prisal made two circuits of Ireland and ravaged the
coast. The daring and the successes of Captain John
Paul Jones in British waters are familiar to every
American schoolboy. On February 6, 1778, at the bar
of the House of Lords, it was shown that during the
war 733 British vessels had been destroyed by Ameri
can privateers. After our independence had been won
and constitutionally entrenched, in our operations
against France within two years and a half we cap
tured eighty-four French ship?. The defeat of LTn
surgent by the Constellation and the fruitful cruise
of the Enterprise through the waters of the West In
dian possessions of France, even in that earl day
attested our naval strength. The victory of Commo
dore Perry at the battle of Lake Erie, which com
pelled the British to abandon Detroit and the whole
territory of Michigan, still thrills the patriotic youth
of our country. The speedy entry of Captain (after
ward Commodore) sloat into the hay of Monterey
and the hoisting- of the American flag at that point
on July 2, 1846. thwarted British strategy on the Pa
cific. In the Civil War there were repeated exhibi
tions of American skill in naval construction and of
magnificent American gunnery, of which the duel
between the Kearsarge and the Alabama was a splen
did example.
We are essentia.!'}- a maritime people and have lost
none of our capacity for supremacy on the sea. It
was not till [884 that we comprehended the necessity
of the reconstruction of our navy. But in fourteen
years we were enabled, directly or indirectly, to pro
tect our vast coast line on the Atlantic and the Pa
cific, and at an inconsiderable sacrifice to pound the
Spanish navy into submission at Manila and at
Santiago.
The battle of Manila Bay has truly established a
date in history.
THE GOLD PROBLEM IN THE EAST.
I 7 ITH a national treasury filled to overflowing
WITH a national treasury filled overflowing
and with the great banking centers -"'glutted
with money that capitalists, finding no ade
quate supply of investments on earth, arc buying
stocks inflated with wind, the general public of the
East is loudly complaining of a lack of currency. The
situation is peculiar, and the press of that section is
naturally giving a good deal of attention to it.
From all reports it appears there is an abundant
supply of currency for all purposes when gold, silver
and paper are taken together, but the Eastern public
will not use gold. The treasury officials have found
it impossible to get it into circulation. Treasurer
Roberts is reported to have said recently: "The ap
peals for paper are becoming absolutely piteous.
There is a special demand'- from Kansas City and
points in the South and West. The treasury is run
ning over with gold, and we could get several mil
lions more gold than we have if we had small notes
to exchange for it.
. A considerable number of our Eastern exchanges
maintain that the objection to the use of gold is due
to the fact that it is not coined in a way to make it
available for retail trade. The Philadelphia Record,
for example, says:
"Two-thirds of all the gold which has been received
at the Government's coining mill since the establish
ment of the mint in 1792 has been coined into $20
pieces, and two-thirds" of the remainder into $10
pieces. Coins of these denominations tire too valu
able for general use in retail transactions. The pro
portion of $5 pieces and $2 50 pieces struck at the
mints is so small that if a demand should be created
to-morrow for these coins for general use they could
not be obtained.''
While there is unquestionably some force in that
argument, it cannot be accepted as conclusive. In
saying that if there should lie a demand for $5 and
$_' 50 gold pieces the supply is so small they could
not be obtained for general use, the Record itself,
seems to concede that there is at present no demand
for gold of these denominations any more than for
the larger coins. If that be so â if the demand is not
sufficient to make use of the small gold coins already,
minted, it is certainly illogical to conclude that more
cf them are required.
The weakness of the position taken by the Record
and so many other Eastern papers is most conclu
sively shown, however, by the fact that California
uses gold and silver almost exclusively, and there has
never been any complaint here of the manner in
which it is coined. In fact, there would probably be
serious objections to the coinage of $2 50 gold pieces
on account of the smallness of the coin and its conse
quent inconvenience in use.
The Eastern objection to gold seems to be mainly
the result of habit. Cattle brought up on the prairies,
having never seen corn, can hardly be induced to eat
corn until hall starved. Eastern retailers are unac
customed to gold coin and arc afraid of it. It will be
of advantage to the nation, however, to induce them
to use it, even if it takes a little currency starvation to
force them into it. JThe general use of .gold as cur
THE SAM FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, HAY 2, 1599.
rency will tend to hold it in the country when other
wise it might be shipped to Europe. It is to be hoped,
that it will not be long before the East reaches the
California standard in this matter and gold coin be
comes the common currency of the country.
SPEAKER REED AND THE PUBLIC.
WHILE Speaker Reed has not thought it worth
while either to confirm or deny the reports that
he will not serve in the next Congress, the
country generally has reached the conclusion that the
rumors are correct, and, what is more, the prevailing
opinion seems to be that he is to retire not only from
Congress but from politics, and that we are to know
him no longer as a statesman and a party leader.
In no section of the country is the belief that Reed's
public career is closed more dearly asserted than in
New England. Nearly all the newspapers of that sec
tion have treated his retirement very much as if it
were his death, and their comments on the subject
have been in the nature of funereal eulogiums. The
fact that Mr. Reed is said to intend to leave New Eng
land and settle in New York may have something to
do with the dolorous New England view of the sub
ject. Such a removal is doubtless equivalent in their
judgment to a departure from the land of the living.
Certain incidents attending the ex-Speaker's de
parture from New York for Europe strengthen the
belief that he really intends to abandon his Presi
dential aspirations and give up politics, as the New
England papers assert. In the first place when, on
the eve of sailing, a reporter told him the public is
interested in the rumors about him and would like to
.â¢mow whether they are true or not. Reed replied:
"The public may be interested in me, but I have no
interest in the public." In the second place while
Croker, who sailed on the same -hip. was attended by
a host of people who came to bid him good by. hardly
any one paid any attention to Reed. fie went away
like an ex-President rather than a Presidential can
didate.
In spite of all these evidences that the strong man
has abandoned the arena of his life's best work, he
may yet return and become a more potent factor in
our political affairs than ever. The whirligig of time
brings about many changes, and not infrequently they
occur with great rapidity. Mr. Reed represents so
much of what is- best and wisest as well as what is
strongest in Republican leadership it is not likely he
will be permitted to remain aloof from the great
struggles that are coming in the near future.
It is to be noted, moreover, that many Democrats
who once denounced him as "Czar" Reed have not
hesitated now to declare that his retirement from
politics is a national misfortune. He represents, in
fact, the conservative anti-imperial business element
of the nation in a higher degree than any other states
man. with the possible exception of the President
himself. McKinley's administration has been so suc
cessful in home politics and so victorious in foreign
affairs that his re-election in 1900 i- a foregone con
clusion, but Reed i- not too old a man to be hi- sue-
-â â â  -â â â 'â â â  â â Â'.-< 5* â â ".â -â 
( essor, ami events may so shape themselves as to
render his return to Republican leadership inevitable
within a few years at most.
BOSTONION BRYANISM.
ALL the world knows that Bostonian ideas arc
classical and metaphysical, and that Bryanism
is but a declamatory demagogucry. therefore
it is to be expected that a Bostonian Bryanism would
be a demophysical metagogucry expressed classically
in declamatory' terms. That expectation has been
realized. George Fred Williams of Boston has for
mulated the principles of Bryanism into four dogmas,
ows:
1. if monopoly has occupied the avenues of com
munication the people can dispossess it.
2. If the land has passed into tin- hands of idlers,
who buiid palaces from th.- pennies of labor, we
can send these Idlers to tip plow.
'... If industry's Implements are possessed by an
oligarchy â * democracy can resume possession.
4. if wealth can buy Legislatures, wo can legis
late for ourselves.
These dogmas come to us without anything in the
way of an explanatory chart, diagram or appendix,
and the Western mind therefore will have some diffi
cult} in understanding the problems of life, mind,
politics and reform involved in them. It is worth
while, however, for all citizens to make an attempt to
penetrate their true inwardness and discern, if pos
sible, the thingness of the vacuity in which they are
immersed, for there may be a bug in their mazes.
The first dogma appears to be a Bostonian way of
saying democracy can take possession of transporta
tion lines if it feels like it, and may be regarded as a
declaration in favor of Government ownership of rail
ways. The second is very obscure. It may be an
occult expression in favor of the nationalization of
land, or it may be a proposal to establish a system
of compulsory plowing on the part of the Astor boys.
The third dogma, maintaining the rights of democ
racy to take up "industry's implements," is a stag
gerer. It seems to imply that Democracy might go
to work, but such a move within the camp of Bryan
ism is inconceivable, and the solution ot the problem
must be left, therefore, to the hazard of a guess.
The last of the four is what is known in the West
a- a "corker," but doubtless it would be recognized in
Boston by some other name. "If wealth can buy legis
latures, we can legislate for ourselves." Apparently
that means that if wealth buys legislation every man
shall become a legislator and get iii- share of the
bcodle. If it mean not that, then it must have some
mysterious reference to an omnibus referendum,
popular initiative and imperative mandate.
Whatever views may be taken of them, it is clear
these dogmascontain in their illucid depths the esoteric
tenets of Bostonian Bryanism. While to outsiders
they may seem but another attempt to "scrut the in
scrutable and poss the impossible," they are utter
ances of the oracles of the dollar dinner, and beneath
their froth may be deeply fraught with the force of
tate. It is probable they will meet us at Philippi in
1900, and it behooves us to take note of their ghostly
threats.
The appointment of the Board of Health by Gov
ernor Gage requires no special criticism. The gen
tlemen chosen may be competent, but the manner
of their selection proves the indifference of the execu
tive to the best interests of the State and of his party.
Dr. 'Barbat, one of the appointees, is the family phy
sician of Daniel M. Burns. Dr. W. B. Coffey is
chief surgeon to. the Market street railway combine.
The complete subserviency of Governor â  Gage to
railroad influences is now freshly illustrated, but has
been long predicted and virtually confessed.
The Labor Council of this city has a suspicion that i
Governor Gage is trying to sell it a gold brick" in
reference to the- appointment of a Labor Commis
sioner. When the Labor Council of San Francisco
becomes better acquainted with Governor Gage the
suspicion will be transformed into a certainty.
United States Senator Perkins has added another
reason to the many which entitle him to the kindly
consideration of the people of the State. He has
made Governor .Gage his enemy.
RANCH AND RURAL LIFE
An inquiry was addressed to the question department of this paper
about the value of irrigation i" prevent injury to orchards and vineyards
by frost. The utility of irrigation for that purpose is demonstrated. The
reason of it lies in the temperature of the water. The water of springs,
which is spoken of as ice cold, in hot weather, has a temperature of 60 de
grees. When the air has a temperature of 100 degrees the water is 40 de
grees colder than the air. a difference at once perceptible in its grateful
coldness when used to quench thirst. But' when the temperature of the air*
has fallen to 32 degrees the water remains at 60 and is 28 degrees warmer
than the air. Water flowing in streams or impounded in reservoirs is pen
etrated, more deeply by the sun's rays and therefore warmed more pro
foundly than the ground and is also warmer than the air at the frost
point. Now, if this water, warmer than the air, is set flowing through an
orchard or vineyard, being warmer, than the air, it raises the local tempera
ture, raises the dew point and averts frost.
The orchards of the British isles cover only 226,059 acres.
The navel orange leads all others in the English market, and the Eng
â¢ lish Fruit Grow says it has a great future. ;
The jute bag supply from merchants who import from Calcutta 'and
the jute mill at ' San Quentin amounts to 41.000,000 bags. If all these can
be m_fde of flax tow it will be seen that flax will become a leading farm
crop in this State.
The Japanese soy bean, used in the German army ration, is grown suc
cessfully in Kansas. it is put on kind after wheat or oats, in the same
year, and its value is $4 60 per acre net. No doubt it would yield a greater
value in California.
Remember that the foundation of good living and of good cookery is
good eggs. Ten average eggs weigh a pound and they have as much nu
triment as a pound of porterhouse steak. Good eggs are produced only by
hens fed on good, pure, untainted food.
The investigation of the California State Board of Trad" into irrigation
by pumping demonstrates that it will give a <rreat impetus to small farm
ing, and that small landholders will.be increased in Dumber by its use.
It will also directly aid truck farming, diversification of crops, and the
adornment of rural homes by shade trees and flowers. All these things go
to the increase of the profits and the pleasures of rural life, and the Board
of Trade should continue to preach the gospel of the pump.
A FAIR LITTLE WORLD OF MY OWN.
I've a ' fair little world of my own,
A place where I enter alone
With fancy to stray
O'er her beautiful way,
And list to her musical tone.
O! she shows me such wonderful dreams,
And I see such radiant gleams
Of all that is bright. â¢
And all that is right;
We drift over wandering streams.
fOyer streams with a murmur so sweet
No one can their music repeat.
1 find not a word
To f 11 what I've heard
From voices whose echoes were fleet.
And so oft when with fancy I flee
1 turn to look backward, and see
The charm that sin- lends
To faces of friends
Who live not in visions with me.
A charm from the flashing In sight
Of 'thoughts that with love are alight,
lake halos they fall
With grace over all
And linger with tenderness bright.
Away from the tumult and drone.
From throngs in which one is alone,
The shailows uplift.
And restful tides drift,-
In that fair little world ef my own.
MARY FRENCH MORTON.
STATE NOTES.
Mr. Flynn of Red Bluff proposes to give a comprehensive trial to the
Australian salt bush as a forage plant in arid situations. From the study
so far made of it he believes it will convert land at present waste and
useless into valuable pasture.
Talk of th" high price of meat, it is nothing to the "price paid in the
flush times. The Bee says that at Sutter's Fort there is the picture of a
grizzly hear that was killed in the tules near Sacramento in early days. The
animal weighed 1400 pounds and the hunter who killed it sold it to a Sac
ramento butcher for $1000. The butcher dressed the meat and retailed it
and it brought him $1700, so thai he cleared $700 in the transaction.
The vine hopper is reported to have appeared at Fresno. Last year the
hopper did not do much damage and absence of the pest is now attributed
to the dry weather.
Last week ripe strawberries were shipped from Florin, Sacramento
County, to Sail Lake City, Portland, Or., Seattle and Spokane. The prices
obtained vere $8, $4 50, $3 T.". and $8 per crate. It is of record that Florin
strawberries have been shipped to Maine and arrived in good order.
Th.- Bee Is of pinion that Placer County fruit growers will wear dia
monds this year. The shipment of cherries and strawberries from New
castle" has already begun and the crop of peach plums and pears is very
promising.
Elwood Cooper, the Santa Barbara olive grower, used to spend $7000 a
year spraying his olive trees to keep down the black ncale. Now he
doesn't spend a cent, but turns the Rhizobius Ventralis, an Australian
lady bug, loose and they eat the scale and smack their lips for more.
The Cutting Packii* Company has built a cannery in Santa Ana and
will put up apricots, string beans, blackberries, plums, asparagus, peas,
strawberries, tomatoes, yellow free peaches, yellow cling peaches and rasp
berries. It is a good indication to see more attention given to the canning
of vegetables. f--
The Oroville Citrus Association started in 1886 with $20,000 paid up capi
tal and put in twenty aires of navel oranges. The stockholders have since
paid in $4000 assessments and now have an orchard of sixty acres, all in
navels, valued at $60,000. The first crop was shipped in 1891. The crop of
1898 netted $9000, or 43 per cent on the original investment, and 15 per cent
on the valuation of $1000 per acre. Let it be remembered abroad that Oro
ville is in Butte County. Northern California, nearly in the latitude of Chi
cago.
The first full carload of California fruit shipped East this year went
by passenger train on the 25th of April. It consisted of cherries and the
Pringle apricot. â¢:. .
A band of 500 sheep were sold at Anaheim for export to Wyoming, at
$10 a head.
During March J. S. Leathers sold to the Knights Landing Creamery
13,356 pounds of milk from twelve coows, for which he was paid $122 82 and
80 per cent of the skim milk.
The ranch property of the late John Boggs, in Glenn and Colusa coun
ties, is appraised at $326,537, and his total estate at $405,037. It was all
gained in prudent farming.

TO CORRUPT THE
SUPERVISORS
Plan for a "Cork
screw" Electric Line.
â â
The attempt that Is being made by the '
Market Street Railway Company to se- :
cure permission from the Board of Super
visors to construct and operate an elec
tric line along Sixth, Taylor, Geary, Grant '
avenue and Bush street lias reached a
condition that bids fair to Involve . the
board in a scandal of sufficient propor
tions to throw a strong light on the meth
ods employed by the giant corporation to
attain its ends.
It is asserted, and the assertion is
backed up by evidence, that the permis
sion prayed for is worth at least $50,000 to
the corporation, which would mean, of
course, a corresponding loss to the city
It is pointed out by the same authority
that the corporation can well afford to
and will if necessary, expend $20,000 lri
purchasing the votes of Supervisors in
favor of granting what is asked. if this
or any other sum ol money is expended
in a successful attempt to corrupt the
members of the board the result will be
that the corporation will be granted gratis
the right to erect electric poles along the
streets named; will be confirmed fn Its
title to franchises now held to be forfeit
ed; will be granted new franchises free of
charge, and will be put in a position to
make worthless to the public the valuable
Geary-street franchise, which will revert
to the city in 1902.-
As a. means for the t acquirement of all
these things without cost to, itself the
Market Street Railway Company has
asked for permission to build a "cork
screw" electric road to traverse the
streets heretofore named. A representa
tive of the corporation recently ap
proached a member of the board with a
so-called explanation of the reasons for
asking such extraordinary concessions.
"Our company does not want to build
this road," he said, "but we are willing to
do so because the down-town business
men want it. The Post-street. Kearny,
Mission anil Market streets lines now han
dle all of the traffic that would be carried
by the new road, so you see we will be out
just what it costs us to build and operate
the new line. But the business men want
; it, and we are willing to shoulder the ex
pense in order to satisfy them."
The argument was so convincing that
. the Supervisor to whom it was addressed
turned to one of his associates with the
remark: "These fellows must take us for
a lot of 10-cent schoolboys."' *
The Geary-street franchise is one of the
most valuable in the city, and when it re
verts to the city in 1902 it will be sold to
the highest bidder. By granting the
Market-street company at this time per
mission to erect poles and operate an elec
tric road on the street, from Taylor street
to Grant avenue, would be equivalent to
giving that corporation a vested right in
the franchise and would make it practi
cally worthless to any company other
than the Market-street monopoly.
As proof of the fact that the scheme on
foot is to i;. control of the Geary-street
franchise, as well as other valuable con
cessions asked for, the manner in which
the corporation's representatives treated
a suggestion made by si vera) members of
the Board of Supervisors is cited. It was
snggested that Instead of turning down
Gearv street from Taylor, tin proposed
road he extend* d another Mo, on Tay
lor street and that it then turn down
Post street to Grant avenue. This propo
sition was rejected, in spite of the fact
that the corporation is seeking permis
sion to transform the Fost-Streel cable
road into an electric road, and would thus
be enabled to save the building of four
blocks of electric line on Geary street.
It Is apparent, therefore, that th* cor
poration considers die occupation of
Gearv street important in a measure
wholly at variance with the unwonted
philanthropy that is alleged as a motive
for building the proposed road.
The request of th*- Market Street Rail
way Company is now in the hands of
the Street Committee of the Board of
Supervisors and has been referred hy it
to the City and County Attorney for an
opinion as to the effect the granting <.f
the request would have on existing fran
chises. Those who are miliar with the
methods of the corporation declare that
upon the report of this committee will de
pend the question of whether the com
pany will attempt to gain Its ends by the
purchase of votes.

â 
HEAVYWIND HAMPERED HARES
GLEN ROY WENT TO DEFEAT IN
THE FINAL.
j . '
A Large Crowd Watched the Sport
and Betting Was Far Above
the Average on Dog
Races.
Yesterday was a day of short undtfs at
Union Coursing Park. In the second
round of the unfinished reserve stake
eleven of the thirty-two courses were de
cided in favor of the lesser dog, and in
Ihe ialt* i part of the game nine oi the
dogs r..ttd below par rewarded their
backers with more than the money 111
--vested. The greatest fail of the day oc
curred when Clifton Lass, after chasing
the rabbit for nearly five minutes, was
given trie flag against Little Joker, a 6 to
1 favorite. The course in which this oc
curred was exciting and both dogs ran to
a standstill] Clifton Lass fell just before
the en 1 and was withdrawn for the day.
A strong wind continued throughout the
afternoon and none of the hares endured
very long. In the third round the sport
livened, however, and the spectators
watched several fairly long match- - and
also some good grand stand work by
Black Bess and Sportsman. The betting
throughout the day was extremely heavy
and a iarge bulk of money was played on
short euders. The crowd was above th.
average.
SouUr-tte took first money. In the final
she went to the slips with Glen Roy, und
the betting on the pair was even. In the
sixth rojno Glen Roy met Forget. The
latter dog led for several lengths; when
the hare swung suddenly in Glen Roy's
favor, giving nim the advantage of a
pretty pick-up. Rollicking Airs was de
feated by Glen Roy after having had
man;, hard and gruelling chases He was
on Sunday's card, as well as Saturday's,
and â¢a*- work of three successive days
told heavily and robbed him of his
chances for the stake wfien the filth
round was run down. Candeiaria showed
up in good form and surprised the talent
by lasting until the fifth round.

POLICE OUTSHOOT STUDENTS.
Oakland's Blue-Coated Sharpshooters
Defeat the University of Cali
fornia Team.
The Oakland Police Department's team
of sharpshooters, captained by ,T. Mor
rison, defeated the University of Califor
nia team, captained by O. W. Julian, nt
Shell Mound yesterday, by a Score of 411
to 404. Each man was allowed ten shots
nt the military target, the U. C. boys us
ing the Springfield rifle and the police
men the Winchester. Ihe former were
handicapped by the unavoidable absence
of three of their best shots, but they did
remarkably well, all . tiuoÂ§3 -considered.^

THE TALE OF A BOILER.
How Robert Collan Fared at the
Hands of a Usurper.
Robert Collan was treated at the Har
bor Hospital yesterday for a broken nose
and a lacerated wound of the scalp.
Robert has been making his home in a
boiler which lies, in a vacant lot on
Steuari street for some time past. After
a day spent in picking up a living ho re
tires to his couch In the tar corner of the
boiler and there sleeps the sleep of the
just.
Sunday night Robert spent in revelry,
and yesterday morning when he returned
to his boiler home he found another ten
ant in possession. There was an alterca
tion, and angry words were followed by
blows. Robert had his nose broken but
finally ejected tin- other man. The latter
then began throwing rocks, with the re
sult that Robert's scalp was laid open.
The usurper then fled and Robert hied
himself to the Harbor Hospital to have
his wounds dressed.

P. TULLY ASPHYXIATED.
Found Dead in Bed With the Gas
Turned On After a Protracted
Spree.
Patrick Tuliy. machinist, was found
dead yesterday morning- in his room at
543 A Castro street. The air In the apart
ment was heavy and stifling with the
fumes of Illuminating pas, and the stop
cock of the pas fixture was turned on.
Tully was a machinist .771 years old and
had been drinking heavily for some time
past. He was very much intoxicated last
Sunday night when he went to his bed,
and his friends are of the opinion that
his death was accidental, and that the
gas escaped because the stopcock was
loose and had been turned off and on
again inadvertently by me deceased.
No writing was found in his room to
show that lie had contemplated suicide,
and he had made no threats previously
of that kind. An Inquest will be held this
morning.

RING AT OAKLAND HARD HIT
ADOLPH SPRECKELS CAPTURED
THE DEWEY HANDICAP.
Joe Ullman Beaten by Rosormonde
for Mayday Handicap Wy
oming Proved a Sur
: * prise.
â¢
Dewey day saw a perfect fusillade of
coin in the betting ring at Oakland track,
and the bookmakersâ flrstâ viewed
with well-defined glee the bombardment.
Th.*- California Jockey Club made no mis
take when it reserved Mayday for its
own. the attendance being one of the
largest of the passing season. Backed
horses were prominent at tho finishes
throughout the afternoon, and the ma
jority of books suffered . severe losses.
Not all favorites won, for of seven Pelx
otto, San Tomas, Joe Ullman and Cas
take went down to defeat.
The Dewey handicap, the spreadeagle
affair of the campaign, did not turn out
a very warlike affair. Ihe distance was
one mile and a quarter, and the 7 to 1"
favorite, Adolph Spreckels, carrying 11"*
pounds and Piggott on his back, won with
consummate ease. Cavallo. a 00 to 1 out
sider, succeeded In beating the heavily
played, second choice, Merops, for the
place.
Then followed the Mayday handicap, at
a mile, which proved a much more spirit
ed and sharper conflict. It also resulted
ii* the complete rout of the favorite, Joe
Ullman. Latter, with John Reiff at the
points, was nearly knocked over the fence
at the first turn, falling back to last posi
tion. After this mishap the Duke A
Wishard mystery made up an acre of lost
ground, reaching the wire in time to be
led out half a length by Rosormonde.
Burkes mare was 7 to 1 in the betting
and made all the running.
A very warm favorite was bowled over
in the opening sprint. I'eixotto, with
Bullman â¢on his back, closed a 3 to 10
chance, but failed to have his usual sup
ply of speed aboard, finishing third.
Bessie Lee, a 7 to 1 shot, ridden by Char
ley Macklin, won ridden out from Widow
Jones. . .'y . .. .-
"White Hat" McCarty's orange colors
were carried to the fore in the two-year
old scramble by May Gertrude, a full
sister to Belle Bovd. The ring laid 5 to 1
about the Ally, and at the wire Narvaez
landed her a winner over Gllssando by a
length. The favorite, San Tomas, ran
as if green, but should do later on.
The seven-furlong run for maidens was
made the medium of a terrific plunge on
Mlgar. The big bay colt had Piggott
up, and backed from 8 to 5 down to 7 to
1". won in a walk. Sylvan Lass, poorly
ridden by Romero, finished on the outside,
taking the place from Ed Lanigan.
When a hunter drew a loaded shotgun
from a wagon muzzle-end first, and later,
when one barrel went off, taking one of
his arms, he excused himself by saving
he "Didn't know it was loaded." Johnny
Reiff rode Castake in the mile and a six
teenth run and handled the gelding just
as lf â" he didn't know the public backed
him." In and out *f several pockets,
with nothing in front of him, his mount
was downed at the wire by Wyoming, the
second choice. Don Vallejo, a 100 to 1
shot, finished third.
The final six-furlong sprint fell to Mo
corlto. the even money favorite. Dolore
led into the stretch, when Jenkins fool
ishly eased her up, only to l"- defeated by
a head.

HURT IN THE PARK.
Attorney John C. McKee Thrown
From His Bicycle and Seri
ously Injured.
There was only one serious accident
yesterday In Golden Gate Park to mar
the pleasure of the thousands that
availed themselves of a day's outing. At- ,
torney John C, McKee of 809 A Bush
street, whose office is at 230 Montgomery,
while returning from the Cliff Louse on !
his bicycle In company with his friend,
Attorney J. F. Fassett, was run over by
a vehicle at Fell and Baker streets, just
inside the panhandle of the park, and se
riously hurt. Dr. Niemeyer, the phy
sician at the Emergency Hospital, says
that his condition is exceedingly serious
and that his chances of recovery are
about equal.
The collision was accidental. McKee
was attempting to gel ahead of one ve
hicle when he ran Into another, lie was
thrown from his wheel and run over. Tiie
police patrol at the Golden Gate Park sta
tion happened to be passing at the time
and the unfortunate man was taken to the
Emergency Hospital. It was found that
he had sustained a severe fracture of tin
skull at the base of the brain on the j
right side of the head, and he also had
several severe contusions on the face
over the left eve. His Injuries were at
tended to by Dr. Niemeyer and Dr. A.
11. Sweeney and he was removed to his
home. Mc Kee Is a middle aged man. He .
came here from Kansas several years
ago, and it is said that he was a Judge
back in his native State. The names of
the people who were In the vehicle that
ran over McKee could net be learned,
as no arrests were made. y_

MEN MATCHED BY THE
SAN FRANCISCO CLUB
TWO HEAVY-WEIGHT BOUTS OF
TEN ROUNDS EACH.
The National Club Will Give a Box
ing Carnival Open to All Classes
of Local Scrappers.
The San Francisco Athletic Club com
pleted arrangements last evening for its.
fistic entertainment whlfh will be held on
Friday evening. Commencing with the
next how the club will be represented by
Alex Greggalna as the official referee,
who, with the assistance of two judges,
will decide all contests. The event of the
evening will be "an eight-round bout be
tween Bob Jones of Los Angeles and
Chick Haywards of Portland. It is said
that Jones whipped Dan Long in one
round, and that it took big Jeffries three
rounds to accomplish the same trick.
Haywards is thought well of, as he de
feated Van Iluskirk recently rather eas
ily. The preliminaries will consist of
eight-round bouts between Gus Koster
nnd Sam Bruit Paddy Maloney and Ed
die Toy and Charles Thurston and M. Mc-
Clure.
The game will commence at S o'clock
sharp, so thai the spectators can get j
away at a reasonable hour.
The Western Club was seriously think- j
ing of matching Neill, who defeated Tra- j
ccv recently, against George Green, but
as" the latter is too heavy and too x
perienced for Neill, the friends of the last j
named pugilist are advising him to seek
smaller gam* before tackling the artful j
George. Doubtless the Western Club will
hunt up some welter-weight among the
local fry who will prove a better drawing |
card than Green.
The National Club proposes to give a
four days' boxing carnival in the near
future. Manager Gibbs is of the opinion
that a series of contests between local
middle, welter and light weight profes
sional pugilists (as all boxers who contest
for a monetary consideration are profes
sionals) would be well patronised by the
sporting fraternity of this city, who arc
anxious to see what kind of talent can be
got from among the boys who are now
boxing for small sums at the "side
street" clubs. Neill; Green. Corbett. Shar
key. Chovnski, Hawkins, Smith and other
professional fighters of note boxed us
amateur:-, in tins city and sprang to fame
from the ranks â¢>!" the common herd. The
Intention of the National Club managers
is to bring out the latent talent nd to
match the winners of the different classes
against Eastern men. The idea is con
sidered to be good, and whether It will
prove a success financially or not the Na
tional Club has concluded to go. ahead
with the scheme. Fuller particulars â¢con
cerning the nival will be announced
in a few days.
Billy Smith, the "Mysterious" crank, is
ir.aithed to light a welter-weight in Salt
Lake; but it is safe to say that when the
Salt Lakers have had a sample. of Smith's
ways of doing business, they will remem
ber what the San Francisco papers have
said about him.
Billy Lavigne Is not going to land.
The managers of the club in which lie ex
pected to Tight Kurge have been informed
of how Lavigne pulled the wool over the !
eyes of the sporting people of this city,
ainl they wisely concluded not to send
him expense money and declared the
match off. Lavigne, however, has seen
his best days, and to win he must take
on small game in future.

Two Children Lost.
Two children, both six years of age,
were reported to the Golden Gate Park po
lice station yesterday afternoon as having
been lost In the park. 7!,. were lost by
their parents in the large crowd of little
ones that thronged the children's play
ground. Their names were Clarence Gil
son of 1662 A Mission street and Willie
Dodge of yi7 Howard street.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
Snow if fatting generally over Utah, Nevada
and Northern Arizona The pressure has ri."?en
rapidly over the northwestern portion of the
country and the storm which was central Sun
day night over the desert section of- Nevada la
now well to the east of the Rocky Mountains.
Th.- temperature has fallen decidedly over
Utah. Nevada and extreme Eastern California.
At Bait Lake City the temperature has fallen
to the freezing point and still colder weather
may be expected on Tuesday.
Rain has fallen In the extreme southern por
tion of California. Light frosts occurred this
morning In the San Joaquin Valley and are
likely to occur again Tuesday morning.
The following maximum wind velocities are
reported: Portland. 26 miles per hour from the
west; Eureka, 3* southwest; Independence, 40
northwest, and Yuma, 72 northeast.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. May 1.-The market to-day
suiYcred from general liquidation and confident
operations by the bears. The passage of the
Ford franchise tax bill early unsettled the
market because of the weakness displayed by
the stocks most concerned. Supporting: orders
by parties largely identified with various prop
erties and covering by the shorts steadied the j
market for a while and some recoveries oc
curred. A renewal .of large offering* brought
prices down in a semi-demoralized fashion and
the closing was at the lowest for practically
all Stocks. Stop-loss orders were met in a
variety of stocks and the pressure continued to
the end. The pronounced strength In sterling
exchange -u-uuscu an expectation I" some quar
ters of exports of gold, but although opinion
as to the probability of this was divided there
were enormous sales of both accounts in the
market. Money ranged up to 8 per cent, but
closed at half that figure. The sales by Lon
don of copper stocks recently was one reason
for the strength of sterling exchange, but the
more Immediate cause was the transfer of the
Spanish Indemnity warrants for 120.000,000 to the
representative of Spain, who was reported to
have called for instructions as to the matter
of transferring the funis to Spain. This gave
color to the rumor that the transfer had not
been anticipated in the sterling exchange mar
ket, but this, of course, was pure conjecture.
Some authorities were of the opinion that
sterling exchange was unlikely to advance to
a point which would make the lead exports
profitable. While such action might arise
within the next two months there wen those
of the opinion that the recurrence of the neces
sity to finance Industrial consolidations, for In
stance that of the Amalgamated Copper Com
pany, this week would maintain money at a
rate which -would make ll more profitable to
leave the gold here rather than ship in any
quantity abroad. â -.'.
Among the extreme declines which early un
settled the list the local shares were most
prominent, including Consolidated Gas, 14',;
Metropolitan, 12%: Third Avenue, 9; Manhattan,
6 *. and Brooklyn Transit. 6',i. The decline In
gas shares was accompanied by the announce
ment of a cut in prices. Other prominent de
clines were New York' Air Brake, 17. Sugar,
<Â»Â»; Federal Steel, 7: American Tobacco, 5;
Anaconda Mining. 4.J; International Silver,
4* 4 . Continental Tobacco, 4%, and American
Steel and National Steel. 4 each. There wa3
also pronounced weakness in People's Gas.
Tennessee Coal and practically all of the In
dustrials. From the extreme depression there
were but few recoveries, among which was Air
Brake "'â¢; and Metropolitan, Sugar and Third
Avenue over a point each. Among the rail
ways the losses ranged up to 4% in Northwest,
with the average elsewhere about 2. The fresh
outbreak on liquidation toward the close was
associated with the strength of money. To
day being a bank holiday in London there was
no indication of the attitude of foreign specu- i
lators in the local market. Hallway mortgages
participated in the weakness of the day, but
concessions were mainly unimportant.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, May The reported appearance
of chinch bugs and continued dry weather In
the Southwest started wheat firm. July opened
%C higher, at 73%Â®73%c, and advanced to 74c.
Statistics were bearish, however, and the ad
vance brought out free selling in the way of
profit taking, under which July declined to
73% C. The market became strong again on a
renewal of the drought claims from Russia and
reports of delayed seeding in the Northwest.
July rose to 74% c, but realizing set in again
and prices slipped off to 73% c sellers at the
close. Heavy deliveries caused May to rule
relatively weak. The July premium increased
to 2c. Cash demand was Door.
Corn ruled heavy and lower under liquidation.
Claims were made that the largest acreage on
record is being seeded. July closes with a loss
of '.â¢â¢ to %c.
Diminishing stocks and a good cosh demand
â  steadied oats. July closed unchanged.
Large receipts of hops and free deliveries on
May contracts weakened provisions. Pork de
clined 10@12'ic, lard 2%@6c and ribs 5c for
May options.

NOTEâ the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there, are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
! United States Coast â¢ Survey charts, except
when a minus slsn (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane* of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.
NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographic
I Office, located In the Merchants' Exchnnge, is
; maintained ln San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard' to nationality and
I free of expense.
Navigators are cordially. Invited to visit the
J office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce. .*
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 12"th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory. Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, is published
in the morning papers the following day.
C. G. CALKINS,
Lieutenant, U. S. N., in charge.
