Col. Compton Before
Court-Martial.
SOME DAMAGING TESTIMONY
THOSE CHECKS CUT BUT VERY
LITTLE FIGURE.
Evidence of the Prosecution All Ln
and the Defense Will Now
Have a Chance to Be
Heard.
The testimony against Lieutenant Neail
is all in, and to-morrow his defense will
open. Colonel Compton, commanding the
Fourth Cavalry, was the lasl witness of
importance, and his testimony was direct
uui unsparing. 11. said Neall had will
lully deceived him when there was no
cause for his deception and al! the ques
tions from the defense could not alter
the tone of his evidence.
Colonel Compton was called to testify
to the events of tiie post exchange trans
fer ami the non-payment of the dividends.
His evidence was given in.support of thej
charge of conduct unbecoming an officer
ar.d a gentleman.
Early in February Compton hud called
Neall to his offlce and had Inquired why
it was there had been no dividends from
the post exchange. Neall told him there j
were certain checks among the assets
of tiie exchnn/.e and these had to be |
s:er.*'ii by Mrs. Nolan, as administratrix, j
of her husband's estate, as they had been
made payable to Lieutenant Nolan while j
.':â¢â¢ had been in charge of the exchange. |
Neai! was also asked about the turning
over of tii*' affairs of the exchange to
Lieutenant Slavens; who had been ordered
to succeed him! and lie gave the same ex- I
cuse as a reason for not complying with j
the order. Colonel Compton took this j
excuse as a good one. Later on he had '
another talk with Neall on the same sub- !
ject, and Neall gave him to understand
that the affairs of the exchange ere still
in the same position: also that he had
found it impossible to secure the indorse
ment of Mi - Nolan.
These explanations'. ' impton said,
seem*-d to him to be plausible, until he
learned that Neall was short $3500 in his
accounts, and then his investigations
showed him that lie had been deceived.
On N'eali's disappearance i .ompton made
an examination of the post exchange and
found about ?12<X) in the safe. The stew
ard gave him $200 more and there were
cheeks enough over that to bring the total
amount up to over SI7OO. Of the $300 in
cheeks there was about $200 represented
by the cheeks which required the signa
ture of Mrs. Nolan.
"What was then your opinion of the
excuse made hy Lieutenant Neall for not
paying th*' dividends and turning over the
exchange?" was asked of the witness by
the judge advocate, and he answered, "I
believed it was made because he did not
have the mor.ey to turn over."
"Why do you regard his statement as
a falsehood?"
"Because his bonks showed he should
have had about $4900 on hand, and there
was no reason why he should not have
paid the dividends less the $200 repre
sented by the checks that needed to be
signed hy Mrs. Nolan."
Continuing, Colonel * lompton told how
on the morning of March 1!Â» Neall came
to him and said he wished to report back |
from ahsence without leave. He was
told that the mere reporting hack did not
end the matter, and then he -. is told of
the shortages that had been found, and
was asked if he was ready to make resti
tution. He said he was. and then Lieu
tenant Harris, the adjutant. Lieutenant
M* Nair, who succeeded liim as treasurer
of the clv!*; Lieutenant Slaveris, who suc
ceeded him in charge of the exchange,
and Lieutenant Walker, who succeeded
him in command of his troop, were
called in. and each reported the shortage i
in his own department and Lieutenant !
Neall made it good. !
Colonel Compton said further that the
checks needing Mrs. Nolan's signature
could just as well have been turned over
to Lieutenant Slavens when the order di
recting the transfer of the exchange was
made as later, when they were turned
over after Neall had disappeared.
Herman Loeth, the hospital steward.
was recalled to tell that he knew of no
prescription which had been mad.- out
foi- Lieutenant Neall by Dr. Edie, and
then he cave place to Edward Wallace,
steward of the post exchange, who was
wanted ti tell of the transactions of the
exchange before ami just after Neall took
charge. His testimony showed that Neall
was not attentive in his. supervision. He
said further, however, that from the
books it was possible to gain an accur
ate statement of the (fairs of the ex
change. This was a lift for the defense
in its effort to show that all's fault
was neglect under pressure of his duties,
not any real wrong.

GRAPE-NUTS.
A TRUE BRAIN
Can Be Built by the Right Materials.
The statement is made and is sus-
ceptible of proof that brains are built
somewhat as a wall or house is built
nf certain defined and well understood
things.
Wood may be the principal and larg-
est article in a house, but it cannot be
formed Into a house without the nails,
bolts or screws to hold it in shape.
We know that albumen Is the bulkiest
thing used by Nature in building a
human brain; but albumen alone does
not make a brain. It requires the
small [.articles of Phosphate of Potash
(like the nails in the house) to form the
albumen Into what is known as gray
matter in brain and nerve tissue.
The albumen exists in large quanti-
ties in a variety of food.
The Phosphate of Potash is found in
its truest form in certain parts of field
grains. When a man shows brain fag,
or a woman shows nervous symptoms,
it is prima facia evidence that the
Phosphate of Potash is used up faster
than the food supplies it. Therefore
cither quit' the worrying work or get
food Strong in the natural Phosphate
of Potash, to more quickly and. surely
replace the daily loss.
That is the especial errand of the
famous food, Grape-Nuts.
Good, sturdy nerves, and a healthy,
active, tireless brain, will make a man
rich where he is sure to fail when the
brain Is unable to carry out the be-
hests of the mind.
Remember that Phosphate of Potash
as a drug. will not do, for Nature takes
kindly only to such body builders when
presented in food as prepared in
Dame Nature's own laboratory.
Grape-Nuts are to be found in all
first-class groceries, and once tried
the delicious flavor will never be for-
gotten, while the feeling of increased
strength and vigor of : brain and i
nerves will tell its own tale. j
FUMIGATION
VERY BAD FOR
LEATHER GOODS
Crews of Transports
Find That Out.
SHOES, BELTS, HATS RUINED
VALISE AND ITS VALUABLE
CONTENTS DISAPPEAR.
Sick Soldiers From the Grant and
Sherman Landed at Folsom-
St.tot Wharf and Sent
to the Presidio.
The United States transport Sherman
was released from quarantine yesterday
and was docked at Folsom street wharf
by the tug Fearless. The big steamer was
covered with bunting, hut she did not
make any braver showing than did the
smaller craft that brought her across the
bay. The Sherman was not detained ln
quarantine because the case of sickness
thai had occurred on board was over
fourteen days old and there was very lit
tle fear of infection. In the case of the
Grant, however, it was different, as the
victim of smallpox only died a few days
before the ship reached port. The Grant,
therefore, will be detained at Angel Isl
and until Monday next.
There is a vigorous "kick" from the
crew of the Sherman over the manner in
which their belongings were fumigated.
The quarantine officials assert that the !
men were warned not to put any leather
goods among the articles to be fumigated,
and the members of the crew assert that
no such order was issued. Be that which
ever way it may, one thing is certain, and
that is over half the crew of the Sherman j
had to walk from the fumigating house
back to the steamer barefooted. Many of
the men had taken advantage of the op
portunity to buy "cheap shoes" (thai Is,
Srst-class shoes made by cheap labor) in
Nagasaki, and these went in with their
baggage and came out worthless. Sweat
bands were eaten off hats, pocketbooks
were ruined and leather belts were turned
Into a crisp mass.
"I wear a No. 7 shoe," Bald A. C. Mont
gomery yesterday, "hut do you think I
could pet my foot into that thing? That
pair cost me $7 in Nagasaki. They are
hand-sewed and of the best material, but
now a child could not get its foot into
them. They are shrunk to a third of their
size and are not worth 5 cents. I had a
cloth belt with leather pockets in it. The
pockets are all shriveled tip and the belt
is useless. One of the soldier boys sent
his purse to be fumigated by mistake.
When the purse came back the soldier had
to cut his money out of it. Well, we're all
out and injured, but I guess that's all the
quarantine officials care."
While the Sherman's passengers were
getting their baggage examined on the
wharf one of them missed ' a valuable
Before and after treatment in
the fumigating tank at the An
gel Island quarantine station.
valise and the police were called in. Ev
ery portion of the wharf and the vehi
cles on it were overhauled and. the
steamer was searched from stem to stern,
but no trace of the valise or Its contents
could be found.
As soon as the Sherman was tied up i
alongside the wharf J. A. Cruickshank
had his belongings taken ashore by the
cabin boy. One valise he was particu- <
larly careful of. because in it was a rare
collection of Spanish coins, secured at
great expense in Manila; a number of
curios and a very valuable collection of
odds and ends from the battle-fields and
other places in the Philippines. Mr.
Cruickshank had occasion to go back
aboard the transport, and when he re
turned to the wharf the valise was gone
Officers Shaw and Ellis of the Harbor po
lice were called in. but the valise had
vanished and a most diligent search re
vealed no trace of it. The customs
searchers were called in and they also
went over the transport carefully, but
without result. Mr. Cruickshank must

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, MAY Â«2, 1899.
NEW COMMANDER FOR
KNIGHTS TEMPLAR.
'
>JoÂ§epl? C. Campbell Elected to Preside?
. Over* Golden Sate Commandery. I
LAST night there was an attendance of about 120 members and several
visitors at the stated assembly of Golden Gate Commandery No. 16,
Knights Templar, in Golden Gate Asylum. The principal business of
the evening was the election of officers for the ensuing term. Joseph
C. Campbell of Reddy, Campbell & Metson was elected eminent com
mander to succeed Charles L. Patton. and the other officers chosen are:
Robert McMillan, generalissimo; Harvey D. Loveland, captain general; R. B.
Moore, senior warden; R. W. Campbell, junior warden; 11. .1. Sadler, treas
urer, and William T. Fonda, recorder. The most prominent of the visitors
was Right Eminent Grand Commander John F. Merrill.
After the close of the business there was an adjournment to the banquet
hall, where a choice collation was served and there was speaking in an in
formal way. brief addresses being made by Sir Knight Merrill, the officers
elect and the incumbents. The new officers will be Installed on the -2d inst.
now possess his soul in. patience and trust
to time to bring forth new develop
ments.
Although the Grant is in quarantine a
number of sick soldiers were brought
ashore from her during the day. They
presented a very pathetic contrast to
the holiday attire or the other ships as
they moved about the wharf, some with
their arms in slings, some on crutches
and all more or less emaciated. Among
those who were landed by the steamer
McDowell were Sergeant W. N. Kelly of
Company B, First California; Corporal
Ormo, First Tennessee; Henry W. Coul
ter, Tenth Pennsylvania; Edward T. For
tier, John Kenworth and Harry Dyer,
Thirteenth Minnesota; Clyde A. Heuter
and Leo M. Foster, Fourteenth Infantry:
Horace E. Cooladge and J. dm Donnellen,
Utah Battery"; Thomas Barkly and Clar
ence P. Bates, Tennessee, and Harry
Raphold, Twenty-third Infantry. Ambu
lances from the Presidio were awaiting
them, and as soon as matters could be
straightened out they were removed to
the hosptlal.
Sergeant Kelly says the California boys
are all well and enjoying themselves.
Th*- majority of them are at Negros, but
the battalion to which he belongs is at
Bacolod. The natives were very friendly
when Kelly left, but an outbreak at
Panay was feared. Kelly's relatives were
down to meet him- fact all the sick
men had friends to welcome them home,
and some very pathetic greetings were
witnessed on the wharf.
GAS REDUCED TO
SIXTY-FIVE CENTS
Consumers Benefit by the War Waged
Between Rival New York
Companies.
NEW YORK, May I.â The New York
Mutual Gas Light Company to-day gave
notice thai the price of gas to all Its con
sumers had been reduced from $1 10 to 65
cents per thousand feet. The Consolidated
Gas Company also announced a similar
reduction.
William P. Purdy, as the secretary of
the New York Mutual Gas Company, ad
mitted that the reduction was the result
of a war between the Standard Gas Com
pany and the Consolidated Gas Company
and its ally, the Mutual Gas Light Com
pany.
"It is the outcome," said he. "of the
war that has been waged against the
Mutual Company and the Consolidated
Company since the Ist of December by
the other companies. The Mutual Com
pany is going to do as well by its cus
tomers as any other company for its
patrons. The proposed municipal owner
ship of gas .plants has nothing to do with
the cut. It is simply a question of light."
As a result of the rate war Consolidated
gas stock declined thirteen points on the
Stock Exchange, where almost 22,000
shares were dealt In. The other local gas
stocks are not listed on the Stock Ex
change.
There were rumors that the Standard
Gas Company would reduce its rates, but
at the office of the president, Russell
Sage, this was denied.
GREAT NAVAL
PARADE ON
THE DELAWARE
_r> l i T-_ .â  Â I
Coghlan Reviews the
Ships in Line.
ALL IN HONOR OF DEWEY
PENNSYLVANIA FOLLOWS CALI
FORNIA'S LEAD.
In Other Eastern States the Anni
versary of the Battle of Ma
nila Caused No Great
Demonstrations
PHILADELPHIA, May 1. â Dewey
day was celebrated in this city with im
posing pomp and ceremony. Governor
Stone, by proclamation, made the day
a holiday in this State on the recom
mendation of the Legislature. He was
not invested with the power to make
j the event continuous, so greater efforts
were extended to render the exercises
i the more auspicious. Public institutions
; were open for business as usual.
The most important event was the
< naval parade on the Delaware River,
which was reviewed by naval and civic
dignitaries. The harbor presented a
I beautiful spectacle, innumerable flags
and. white bunting, entwined with the
! city colors, blue and gold, being dis
j played in profusion on hundreds of ves
sels. The buildings along the river
front and throughout the city were
i gaily decorated.
The cruiser Raleigh was the chief
object of interest. The warship was
anchored at the extreme turning point
of the line of the naval parade. Next
to her was the revenue cutter Algon
quin and all the available craft were
assembled at League Island.
All the vessels to participate in the
pageant started at a signal from the
navy yard from a point a short distance
below League Island. The parade pro
ceeded up the Delaware River, passing
the Raleigh, Captain Coghlan re
viewing the procession from the bridge
of the warship. As the head of the
procession reached the Raleigh the
cruiser fired the national salute, and
then until the last vessel in the parade
had repassed over on the west side of
the river the salutes from. the Raleigh
were almost continuous.
The Russian naval officers who are
here superintending the construction of
the new Russian warships at Cramps'
participated'"'in the ceremonies, review
ing the parade with Captain Coghlan.
Throughout the State celebrations
were being held in honor of the hero
of Manila Bay.
McKinley at New York.
NEW YORK, May I.â President and
Mrs. McKinley breakfasted early this
morning at the Hotel Manhattan. After
breakfast the President received a num
ber of callers, among them Grenville M.
Dodge. At 9:45 o'clock the President, Ab
ner McKinley and Assistant Secretary
Cortelyou drove to the foot of East Twen
ty-third street, where they boarded a
Government tug and went to the navy-

yard. Mrs. McKinley remained at the
hotel.
The Presidential party, which included
General Dodge, W. W. Wheaton, Sergeant
Rixey, U. S. N.. and Colonel W. C. Brown,
were received on board the Government
tug Nina by Commander J. D. J. Kelly, U.
S. N.,who was delegated to escort them to
the navy-yard by Rear Admiral J. W.
Philip. .The tug reached the yard at 10:10
o'clock and the visitors landed at the foot
of Main street. As the Nina turned into
the Wallabout Basin the guns on board
the Vermont belched forth a salute of
twenty-one guns in honor of the Presi
dent. Rear Admiral Philip, with Captains
Miller, Ide, Farnholdt and Arnold, greeted
the President and his escort when they
came ashore.
President McKinley and the command
ant then led up the yard to the Lyceum
building, in front of which all the officers
on duty at the yard were lined up in full
dress. One hundred marines in command
of Colonel R. W. Huntington faced the
building. As the party approached the
Marine Band played "Hail to the Chief,
and while the marines presented arms, the
officers and spectators doffed their hats.
Admiral Philip introduced each of the
officers to the President, who shook hands
with every one of them. After this the
party was escorted through the stores and
factories and then visited the Yosemite
and Glacier, which they inspected. After
the inspection they were invited to lunch
eon at the commandant's residence.
After luncheon the President and the
other visitors returned to the city on
hoard the Nina, and as the vessel swung
into the East River another salute Of
twenty-one guns was fired from the Ver
mont, on which vessel the President s flag
was flying. ___
SECRETARY LONG'S SPEECH.
He Defends the Policy of the Admin
istration.
BOSTON, May I.â The Essex Club cele
brated Admiral" Dewey's victory 'with a
i banquet at Young's Hotel to-day, at which
j were entertained as guests Secretary of
the Navy Long and two heroes of the
Manila fight, Captain Wildes of the Bos
ton and Lieutenant Commander Reese,
form* rly executive officer of the Olym
pia, besides Command. James R. Self
ridge, formerly of th.- Puritan, and Lieu
tenant Commander F. A. Wilmer.of the
Monadnock.
Secretary Long began ids speech with
a eulogy to Admiral Dewey, and then
paid a graceful tribute to the officers and
the men of the navy. The greater part
of his speech was a defense of the na
tional administration's Philippine policy.
He said in part: ; .
"I believe thai no more unpatriotic ut
terance, or one more unjust to the ad
ministration of the Government of our
country, has been uttered than the state
ment which I see now and then that 'the
treatment of Cubans by Spain, or the
Armenians by the Turks, is not to be
compared with our course toward the Fil
ipinos in wanton and deliberate cruelty.
The mind that can utter such an extrav
-1 agance is so entirely warped that its
judgment Is valueless."
At Chicago.
CHICAGO, May The celebration of
Dewey day was mostly of an indoor char
acter. In a majority of the schools an
hour was set aside for appropriate exer
cises in honor of the flrst anniversary of
the battle of Manila and its. hero. This
evening numerous banquets and enter
tainments were given, including an "ex
pansion" dinner at the Quadrangle Club.
The celebration was brought to a close
with the rendition of the "Manila Te
Deum" at the Auditorium Theater under
the auspices of several society women.
Three hundred voices composed the
j chorus. The proceeds of the entertain
ment will be.' donated to the Chicago Ma
ternity Hospital and Training School for
Nurses.
At Boston.
BOSTON. May I.â Dewey day was cel
ebrated here by the display of flags over
the schools, public and Federal buildings
and prominent business houses, and also
by receptions held by patriotic societies.
In many of the public schools there were
\ appropriate exercises.
At Kansas City.
KANSAS CITY. Mo.. May The dis
play of flags on public buildings and
many residences and simple exercises at
the schools covered the extent of the
Dewey day celebration here.
At St. Louis.
ST. LOUIS, May I.â Dewey day was
generally observed here to-day by a dis
play of the national colors.

At New Orleans.

ROYAL ARCANUM.
The report of the supreme secretary up
to the Slat of March shows a net gain
during that month of 601 members in the
order. During the first quarter of the year
year there were received 2369 applications,
and the membership at the close of the
quarter was 189,781. The amount paid out
in benefits since the order was estab
lished to the close of the 31st of March is
$50,693,047. The membership in California
was on that date 854.

\
WILL PLACE HER "FRIENDS' ON
THE GRILL.
She Objects Most Strenuously to
Being Divorced After She Has
Been Reunited to Her
Husband.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, May 1.
Mme. Florence Montague- Yates has
caused a Butter among Oakland spirit
ualists by sending word that she will
shortly return and make it warm for
certain people who have forced her to
obtain a divorce from her husband;
The recent letter from Mme. Montague
states that she is in England, where she
went soon after leaving Oakland. She is
exceedingly annoyed with the notoriety
she has met with and which has preceded
her to Europe.
The madame says that the action of
her Oakland friends has entirely misrep
resented her in the eyes of thousands
of people, and thing will satisfy her
but a complete vindication .Mrs. Mon
tague was divorced after her departure
from this city, and after the divorce was
granted she wrote out 1 hat she was living
with her husband, to whom she had. been
reunited in Montreal. She also said that
she had ordered that the proceedings lie
canceled and hat the matter be dropped.
Certain people in Oakland, however, were
determined that the madame should be
divorced, and they paid for the costs of
the litigation.
.lust what the madame will do is proble
matical. The decree is corded, and yet
she says that she did not desire it, is re
united to her husband and was a victim
of others. It is expected that Mme. Mon
tague will return to the coast In about
six weeks.

LIVED THREE
SCORE AND TEN
Dr. Muhr, Editor
and Physician,
Closes a Long
Life of Activity.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, May 1.
DR. HERMANN MUHR. one of
the most prominent German-
Americans in this county, died
at his home in this, city last
night after several months'
illness. Dr. Muhr was for
many years editor of the Oakland
Journal, the German newspaper of
this city. He was a practicing physi
cian for several years in San Fran
cisco and Oakland, and enjoyed a wide
reputation as a writer. For five years
Dr. Muhr was totally blind and his
restoration to sight was considered to
be miraculous.
The doctor was practicing In New Tork when the affliction came upon him
but for the las ttwelve years of his life he had perfect vision. Dr. Muhr
was 71 years of age and leaves a widow, Mrs. Auguste Muhr, two daugh
ters, Mrs. A. Entzmann and Miss Helena Muhr, . and. three sons Adolph
Muhr of Chicago, Hermann F. and Feodor Muhr. members of the firm of
Muhr & Muhr. The deceased was a member of .tho Oakland Turn Verein
and the Press Club.
PROTESTED
AGAINST BEING
RAILROADED
Queer Case of Anna
Colgett.
â 
SAYS SHE WAS NOT INSANE
THREATENS TO HAVE WARDEN \
KEATING OUSTED.
Objected to Summary Imprisonment
in an Insane Ward, and Her
Screams Secured Her
Speedy Release.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, May 1.
Mrs. Anna Colgett, residing at 1004
! Fourteenth street, was locked up In the
i Insane ward at the Receiving Hospital
i fin- a brief period this afternoon, pre
j sumably at the instance of her husband,
but the scene she created, accompanied
i by pitiful screams, resulted in her hus
band relenting and she was soon re
leased. : y
Now she charges Insane Keeper Keat
ing with depriving her of her liberty
without warrant or good cause, and will
bring the attention of the Supervisors to
his alleged unwarranted haste.
Mr. and Mrs Colgett called at the lb
ceiving Hospital shortly after _ o'clock.
The husband handed the keeper of the in
sane a letter from Dr. W. P. Mauzy in
forming him that "this is the case 1
spoke to you about a month ago." The
trio then walked Into the insane ward,
and the keeper, with Mr. Golgett, quickly
left the room, turning the key and leav
ing Mrs. Colgett imprisoned.
"Papa, papa, open that door for God's
J Bake!" cried the woman, beating with all
her might against the door at the same
time. Her screams interrupted the con
sultation her husband and the Insane
Keeper were holding in th*- ball, and re
suited finally in her release. As she
stepped out her face was white with
rage, and she realized apparently the po
sition in which she had been placed, and
she did not propose to be railroaded to
an Insane asylum summarily.
"This is an outrage to society," said
Mrs. Colgett; "and 1 do not propose to
stand it. 1 shall report this matter to the
Supervisors. The idea of suggesting
that I be locked in that room over night.
Why, I would lose my reason. 1 supposed
that I was to come here to be exam
ined by some physician to see what
could be done for the nervous troubl*
from which 1 have been suffering. Such
cruel treatment never was heard of be
fore," and she requested her husband to
accompany her home instanter.
Insane Keeper Keating, smarting under
the woman's rebuke, turned to the hus- j
band and â¢ remarked with anger, "The '
next time you want us to do something
for you. you ought to know what you j
want, and don't come here and make a
fool of me."- Keating's explanation to
the woman â that he had turned the key
in order to hold a consultation with her
husband privately, did not work as a sol
ace, and she insists thai lie shall be re
moved from his position.
"1 have suffered from nervous troubles
the past year," said Mrs. Colgett after j
the scene, "and 1 become very fidgety at
times, but I have never been violent, to
all of which her husband assentlngly
nodded. i
Dr. Mauzy, Mrs. Colgett's physician,
when asked as to tie- woman's mental
condition; manifested reticence. "1 do
not .are to talk about it. It is all a ques
tion of opinion. It is a private case, and
might as well be dropped right now."
insane Keeper Keattng's discomfiture
over tiie incident was great. "I talked
with Dr. Mauzy a month ago about this
very case," said he. "1 was justified in
doing what I did. Mi-. Colgett came with
this letter. He claims she tried to kill
him 0n..-. She couldn't be examined to
day, anyway, so 1 was going to hold her
until to-morrow, but her husband weak
ened, If she had been given a dose of
â¢dope,' or something, we could have held
her here and she would have been quiet."
Mrs. Colgett's husband is a gardener
by occupation, and they have a family of
grown-up children, who will no doubt
lake somebody to task for their mother's
treatment.
The methods in vogue in the insane
wards at the Receiving Hospital were
lately inquired into by the Grand Jury
with considerable scrutiny. The inquisi
torial body meets again next "Wednesday,
ami it is likely thai a partial report will
be forthcoming.
Injured by a Toy Cannon.
OAKLAND, May I.â A juvenile attempt
at a miniature reproduction of Admiral
Dewey's naval feat of a year ago almost '
resulted disastrously to-day, when little
Willie Zabel. aged li years, residing at 660
('lay street, was the "man behind the can
non." Zabel and sonic other boys were
imaginarily bombarding the Philippines
with a toy cannon. At one stage the can- â 
non did not discharge with the desired
promptness and young Zabel looked close
ly into the fuse-hole to see what the
matter was. when the deceptive gun ex
ploded, nearly putting out his left eye. At
the Receiving Hospital Dr. Stratton and
Oculist Thomas attended to his injuries,
and it is feared he may lose the sight of
the damaged eye.

THE THEATERS
By Ashton Stevens.
T\Hl-. Woman Hater," the farce by
David D. Lloyd In which Roland
Reed commenced his second and
last week in the Columbia Theater
last night, is not new to the play
goers of San Francisco. Mr. Reed has
played it here before, and unless 1 am
badly mistaken a local stock com
pany essayed it not very long ago. It
is a farce of no particular novelty. The
woman hater, you are soon given to
understand, is not that at all. but a
veritable Romeo In middle age. who
tin*. ugh misunderstanding and comic
circumstance becomes engaged to no
less than three bouncing widows. He
extricates himself by means of the old
insanity dodge, but winds up in an
asylum, from which he finally emerges
with the woman of his choice.
The first two acts are slow, even un
interrupted by applause, as they were
last night; but the third, which reveal
some rather ingenious complications in
the capture of the woman hater by the
attendants from the asylum, and the
fourth, which shows him fuming in
captivity, mistaking his friends and
fiancees for lunatics, are much funnier,
if indeed they offer little that has not
been done as well and better by other
play writers.
Mr. Reed, throws himself earnestly
into the principal part. Earnestness,
strange as it may sound, is just the
quality that is needed in the principal
aits of most farces. The character
is usually the victim of circumstances,
and the more complicated these become
and the more dead-in-earnest the
amazement ami resentment he meets
them with, the more ludicrous the ef
fect. Unfortunately Mr. Reed's part in
"The Woman Hater" is marred by an
abundance of knowing soliloquy's and
asides that make this mode of treat
ment not always possible. Much of the
time he is engaged in explaining things
to the audience I have noticed that
th.- best plays are those that the audi
ences find out for themselves. Miss
Rush and the other members of the
company furnish a well-ensembled if
not individually notable support.
That this is the last week of Miss Lil
lian Burkhart's engagement at the
orpheum is a matter of regret, not only
to the regular patrons of this vaude
ville theater, but to all admirers of re
fined and distinguished comedy acting.
The fourth week at the Orpheum, and
yet this remarkable young woman is
now, as she was at the first, the best
and most artistic personality in the the
aters of San Francisco! The Orpheum
has given house to many enviable rec
ords, but to none better than this le
gitimate one of Miss Burkhart. I can
think of no finer triumph for any act
ress than to grip the attention of the
Orpheum-goers for four consecutive
weeks, as Miss Burkhart has done, in a
class of work that has little if any nat
ural place in the go-as-you-please of
vaudeville. She has resorted to no vul
garity, to no extreme personal display,
to thing in fact but the cleanest and
keenest of legitimate craftsmanship.
And she has held the attention and ad
miration of all, from the casual play
goer of cultivation who drifted into the
orchestra to the toughest god of the
Sunday-night gallery. Her work will
leave a fragrant memory, a wholesome
tradition for the house that had the
good sense to risk a month of her deli
cacy and refinement. Whether Miss
Burkhart comes back to us as a vaude
ville star, or, as many of . us would
hope, a principal actress in a full
fledged play, her welcome is assured.
This week's piece is an adaptation
from the French of Eugene Pierron
and Adolph Lafferrier, entitled "My
Aunt's Advice;" I have seen the play
somewhere under another name. It
tells of a young bride who, to rid the
house of her husband's chum, pretends
love for him. He discovers the letter
from her aunt which suggested the
hoax, and returns her affected passion
with unexpected good will. The hus
band challenges, and there is a lot of
excitement before the general explana
tion. The dialogue is rather formal ami
Miss Burkhart has fewer individual
chances than in her other plays, but
nevertheless she gets a deal of graceful
fun out of the part and pays another
distinct tribute to her apparently limit
less versatility. Mr. J. K. Macurdy, as
the friend, is still inclined to â¢ fierce
facialism and a vast wobbling of the
eye and a disesteem for real, effortless
humor, but on the whole he is much
easier than in the other plays. The one
disaster is the husband, played by a
Mr. Tracy, who in speech, feature and
action bears a fatal resemblance to
Mr. Jeffries the pugilist.
** - *
The other theaters will noticed in
to-morrow's Call.
CHINESE SLAVE TRAFFIC.
I The regular meeting of the Occidental
| Board of Foreign Missions was held in
j the Mission-house on Sacramento street
yesterday afternoon. In honor of the
j day the Chinese children all wore Ameri
can flags and . opened the exercises by
. singing "My Country, 'Tis of Thee," and
I the "Battle Hymn of the Republic." A
I little Chinese girl played the accompani
ment to both songs on the piano.
Rev. Mr. Miller, a returned missionary
! from Korea, spoke on conditions in that
! country. He said that the -work of the
| missionaries is growing greater every
I year and the field is always open for more
! earnest workers.
Rev. Mr. Ellis, formerly of Boston, but
1 now of this city, read a letter from the
' American Purity Alliance, giving statis
tics in regard to the manner in which the
slave raffia, in Chinese women is carried
on. The Alliance will meet 'In London
next June, and the subject will be taken
iup there and thoroughly discussed. In
the meanwhile the Occidental Board will
j gather all possible statistics and informa
tion regarding this degrading phase of
I Chinese social life. It is probable that
mass-meetings will also Re held in this
city and steps taken toward the stamping
I out of the Â£viL ' ----.--â

NOTICE is hereby given by order of the Board
of Directors of the OCEANIC STEAMSHIP
COMPANY' that a meeting of the stockholders
of said Company has been called by said
Board, to be held on FRIDAY, the second day
of June, A. D. 1899, at eleven o'clock in the
j forenoon of said day, at the principal place of
business of said Company, at the building
where the said Board of Directors usually
meets, namely, at the office of said Company,
| number 327 Market st., in the City and County
of San Francisco, State of California; that
the object of said meeting is to consider and
act upon the proposition that said Company
create a bonded indebtedness of two million
| five hundred thousand dollars ($2,500,000 00). ln
United States gold coin, for the. purpose of
raising money to complete the construction
| of its steamships and their equipment, for
use in the business of thiÃ corporation, and
to purchase and pay- for any other property
within the purposes of this Company: and to
secure the bonded Indebtedness so proposed
to be created by a mortgage upon its steam
end sailing ships and all other property of
said Company now owned or hereafter to be
acquired by said Company.
By order of the Board of Directors of the
Oceanic Steamship Company.
[Corporate Seal.] E. H. SHELDON.
Secretary of the Oceanic Steamship Com-
nary. .

PROPOSALS.
PROPOSALS
To Print, Publish and Distribute the De-
linquent Tax List for IS9B.
OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF THE
Board of Supervisors of the City and
County of San Francisco, April 25, 1899.
In accordance with Resolution No. 2C26
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervis-
ors, SEALED PROPOSALS will be re-
ceived in open session of the Board of Su-
pervisors on MONDAY AFTERNOON,
May 8, 1899, from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for the
printing, publishing and distributing of
the delinquent tax list for the year 1898,
to contain the names of the persons and
a description of the property delinquent
and the amount of taxes and costs due
opposite each name, and a notice unless
the taxes delinquent, together with the
costs and percentage, are paid, the prop-
erty upon which said taxes are a lien
will be sold at public auction. Such
publication to be made and thoroughly
distributed on a business day, at least
once in each week, for three successive
weeks, in a pamphlet fern as a supple-
ment to a daily newspaper of general cir-
culation, published In the city and
county of San Francisco, in the
English language, and . said supple-
ment must be sent and distrib-
uted on the days specified with every
number of the paper issued and distrib-
uted, by carriers, by mail or otherwise.
The first issued to be so distributed on or
before the first day of June. 1899, and the
others weekly thereafter, and to desig-
nate the time and place of sale and in all
respects conform to the provisions of the
Political Code and to be done under the
direction and to the satisfaction of Ed-
ward I. Sheehan, Tax Collector of said
City and County; also, to leave at the Tax
Collector's office not less than one thou-
sand (1000) copies of each issue before any
demand on the Treasury therefor will
be approved. Within one week after the
first publication of . said list fifty (50)
copies thereof, printed upon one side only
of a quality of paper which can be written
upon with ink, to be satisfactory to said
Tax Collector, to be delivered at his office
for use in the office. Also fifty (50) cloth-
bound copies, interleaved, to be delivered
to the Tax Collector's office within two
weeks after the first publication.
The Tax Collector must have the privi-
lege at any and all times during the cxc-
i cution of the said contract of witnessing
the same and giving instructions relative
thereto either personally or through his
! chief deputy, or any deputy he may desig-
i nate. . , â ' -â â  'â 'â 'â  '"â -'â â '
All proofs must be read and submitted
for correction as follows:
Firstâ Proofs must be first read by copy
and corrected by the printer.
Secondâ Submitted in slips to the Tax
Collector for correction.
Thirdâ Revise to be submitted to and
passed upon by the Tax Collector, or hi 3
chief deputy, or deputy; if corrections or
alterations are made by said Tax Collec-
tor, or his chief deputy, or deputy, a cor-
rected revise must be presented to and
approved by him prior to the form being
struck off. and as the work progresses
when a form is struck off a copy of the
same to be filed with said Tax Collector.
Payment to be made as hereinafter pro-
vided, and the bill presented to be veri-
fied by the affidavit of a proper and re-
sponsible person acceptable to the Com-
mittee on Print and Salaries of the
Board of Supervisors, said affidavit to be
made before a notary public in and for
the said City and County, and to set
forth that the contract for printing, pub-
lishing and distributing the Delinquent
j Tax List for the year IS9S, has been fully
! and faithfully executed and accomplished
i in strict conformity to and with these
[ specifications.
The contractor or contractors will re-
ceive a demand upon the Treasury of the
said City and County for the payment of
the contract price in United States gold
; coin, when satisfactory evidence is fur-
l nished the said Committee on Printing
'â  and Salaries that the sale, contract has
been fully and faithfully executed and ac-
! complished in conformity therewith.
A bond in the sum zi rot less than ten
i thousand ($10,000) dollars, and with as
many sureties as the Committee on Print-
ing and Salaries of the Board of Super-
visors may elect to require for the faith-
ful execution of said contract must be
given.
Bidders will' estimate with the distinct
: understanding that the Supplemental De-
! linquent Tax List, if any, shall be pub-
lished for not less than one week, and
that the price to be paid for each assess-
ment on the said Supplemental Delinquent
Tax List shall be not more than twice
the amount bid for each assessment on
the Delinquent List.
The space occupied by prefatory notices,
remarks and explanatory matter will be
paid for per one thousand ems pro rata
wth the average matter in said list. Said
extra matter and pro rata to be com-
puted by an expert to be appointed by the
said Committee on Printing and Salaries
of the Board of Supervisors.
In style and general character said De-
linquent Tax List must -Ã¼bstantially con-
form to and with the list hereunto at-
tached of last year.
Each proposal for printing, publishing
and distributing such list must state:
Firstâ The name of the dally newspaper
published in said City and County in
which it is proposed to so publish such
list.
Secondâ The price to be charged for
each assessment, whether of real estate,
personal property or uoll tax.
Thirdâ The names of the sureties to be
offered in event of receiving the award
herein.
Each proposal must contain or. have at-
tached thereto a sample of not less than
thirty lines of such real estate matter and
personal property matter upon the qual-
ity of paper to be used.
The party to whom this contract Is
awarded will be required, Trior to or at
the time of the execution of said contract,
to pay the cost of advertising this notice
in three dally newspapers, and to also
pay the cost of the publication in the of-
ficial newspaper of the Resolution award-
ing him the contract.. .:
The Board reserves the right to reject
all bids, and will demand good and suf-
ficient bonds in the sum of ten thousand
($10,000.00) dollars for the faithful per-
formance of the contract.

Delinquent Tax List."

NOTICE.
To All Whom It May Concernâ la
hereby given that on the 17th day of April,
1899, the Bakersfleld and Los Angeles Rail-
way Company filed Its application in writing
in the office of the Clerk of the Superior
Court of the City and County of San Fran-
cisco, addressed to the Superior Court of said
county, making application for the voluntary
dissolution of the Bakersfleld and Los An-
geles Railway Company, which said petition
and application stated that at a meeting of
the stockholders of said company on the 13th
day of April, 1899, called for the purpose of
passing upon the question, it was resolved to
have a voluntary dissolution of the corpora- i
tion, and that such resolution was passed by
a two-thirds vote of all' the stockholders and
that all the claims and demands against the
corporation, of every kind, nature and descrip- !
tion, have been fully paid and discharged.
Wherefore, it was ordered by the said Su- '
perior Court upon the filing of said petition i
and application that the undersigned, a3
Clerk of said Court, give a notice of such ap-
plication by publication In some newspaper
published in the City and County of San 1
Francisco, for a period of thirty-five days.
Therefore, all persons having any interest In
the matter of said application or desiring to
oppose the granting of the same are here-
by notified to file in my office, on or before i
the 22d day of May, 1899, any and all objec-
tions which they may have to the granting of i
said petition.
In testimony whereof, I have hereunto set my
hand and the official seal of said court this i
the 17th day of April, 1899.
(Seal). WILLIAM A. DEANE,
County Clerk and ex-Officlo Clerk of the
Superior Court.
By E. M. THOMPSON.
Deputy County Clerk and ex-Officio Deputy
Clerk of the Superior Court.

ALL HEADS AT
THE UNIVERSITY
TO COME OFF
General Reorganiza- !
tion Decided Upon.
BIG SALARY FOR PRESIDENT
HE WILL GET TEN THOUSAND
DOLLARS A YEAR.
WiU Be Expected to Engage a
New Staff of Assistants
and Infuse New
Blood.
The regents of the University of Cali
fornia have not fully determined what
compensation the president of, the insti
tution of learning at Berkeley shall re
ceive under the new and liberal order of
things in prospect, but the sum of $10,000
per annum is so frequently mentioned
that many who are interested in the
change accept that figure as an approxi
mation of the salary. Doubtless the re
gents have exchanged views on the sub
ject, and the promoters of liberal com
pensation reached the conclusion that the
figure named will be acceptable to a ma
jority of the board.
The most enlightened and zealous
friends of the university on the Board of
Regents do not expect that all the de
sired reforms at Berkeley will be brought
about by a change In the presidency of
the institution. Other changes must fol
low in order to obtain the advancement
so long sought. There is a deal of dead
timber in the subordinate chairs that
must be disposed of in some fashion. It is
acknowledged that some of the professors
are useful and progressive, but it cannot
be disputed by the most enthusiastic ad
mirers of the faculty that professors who
ought to be pensioned and retired are re
tained. â¢
It is said that an Eastern man, whose
learning and executive ability commanded
the attention of the regents, was con
sulted as to what plans should be adopted
to eliminate tin- undesirable professors.
He suggested that the new president
should be assured that all the professors
would resign at the end of the year. The
suggestion meets with favor. It may not
follow that a sweeping, radical change all
along the line of subordinate Instruction
would result from the adoption of this
plan, but the new president's task of in
troducing modern methods of education
would be lightened if be were left per
fectly free to select ins coworkers. Capa
ble men, now out of place, might be as
signed to positions where they would be
able to render excellent service in build
ing up the Institution. Men who never
were of any use to the university and
others who have outlived their usefulness
as Instructors could be disposed of with
out public comment or embarrassment to
the president.
The college professors at Berkeley
have not quite given up the hope or the
fear that one of their own set "will be
chosen to succeed Mr. Kellogg. The re
port that the regents have about de
cided to offer the presidency to Professor
Wheeler of Cornell Is not credited by the
friends of William Cary Jones. The in
fluences behind Jones are said to be pow
erful and persuasive.
â  The new regents, including Stephen M.
White of Los Angeles, A. B. Spreckels,
president of the State Agricultural So
ciety; Jacob H. Nefl, Lieutenant Gover
nor; Alden Anderson, Speaker of the As
sembly, and Governor Henry T.Gage, will
participate in the election of President
Kellogg's successor. It would not He sur
prising if White and Gage came up from
the south with a candidate of their own.
it would not be the first time that South
ern California introduced a new man for
a good salary.
The regents are not making haste in
the selection of a new president. The de
cision was reached three years ago that
Mr. Kellogg should retire, but it was
deemed best to withhold the fact from
the public until the Legislature of 1597
adjourned sine die. Long after adjourn
ment the acceptance of President Kel
logg's resignation was 'announced, and
then it was decided to defer the election
of his successor until the Legislature of
1899 adjourned. At the rate of progress
already Indicated the new president will
li* elected about the time the present cen
tury expires.
ARREST OF A PICKPOCKET.
How James Green Celebrated Dewey
Day at Glen Park at Others'
Expense.
A gang of pickpockets took advantage
of the gathering of patriotic citizens at
Glen Park yesterday to ply their vocation
by getting away with other people's prop
erty. Among the number of light-fingered
individuals noticed by the police was
James Green, whose record is well
known to the detective department at the
City Hall. Green was seen by Officers J.
F. Edner, C. H. Taylor and Horace G.
White of the Seventeenth-street station,
who had been detailed by Captain James
W. Gillin to look out for such characters.
As soon as the crowd gathered at some
particular point on the grounds Green and
three pals were seen to push their way
into the thickest of the gathering. It
required but a short time for them to
develop their calling, as two ladiesâ Mrs.
McCradie, who resides on Bernal -Heights,
and Mrs. McLearn, residing at 131 Cali
fornia avenueâ reported to â¢ the officers
that their purses, containing some money,
had been taken out of their pockets by
a young man answering the description
of Green. The officers found him, and on
being confronted with the ladles they at
once Identified him as the man who had
robbed them. He was placed under arrest
and taken to the Seventeenth-street sta
tion, where he was charged with grand
larceny. The other suspects made them
selves scarce when they saw Green- In
the clutches of the officers.
AGAINST POLYGAMY.
Dr. Paden's Address Before the Pres
byterian Ministerial Union.
At the regular weekly meeting of the
Presbyterian Ministerial Union, 920 Sacra
mento street, Dr. Paden read a criticism
yesterday morning on B. H. Roberts'
"Defense of Polygamy," an article writ
ten in the May number of the Improve
j ment Era. a Mormon magazine. The
Ladies' Occidental Board was Invited to
attend.
Dr. Paden considered the article of B.
| H. Roberts seriatim, refuting each and
! every postulate advanced. He showed
', that the Mormons are now practicing
polygamy openly. "Ever since statehood
has been acquired," he claimed, "the Mor
. 1 mon women have taken the names of
their husbands, something which Was not
formerly done."
Dr. Paden then disproved the arguments
' â¢of Roberts, which trace the justilication
of Mormonism to the Bible. "If the polyg
amy of David, Abraham and Solomon was
right It would be equally as good logic
'. to say all the other sinful and vicious
practices mentioned in the Bible were also
: right." The latter portion of Dr. Paden's
address was devoted to Solomon and' his
i many wives. _. .-

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AY 2, 1899.
GRAND JURY
STIRRED BY
DISSENSIONS
Foreman Murdock Is
Not Popular.
CRITICISM OF HIS METHODS
â 
CREDITED WITH A LACK OF EF
FECTIVENESS.
Members Have Discussed the Advis
ability of Taking Steps Toward .
the Selection of a New-
Leader.
The complaints made last Thursday by
a number of business men who had been
summoned to appear before the Grand
Jury as witnesses and who were kept
waiting for hours before being called to
testify have had the effect of bringing to
the surface tho details of Internal dissen
sions in the Grand Jury that are held
accountable for the poor results that have
attended the work of the jury during the
past four months. Many of the members
do not want to rest under the imputation
that they have taken no Interest ln their
work when, as they claim, every effort
on their part has been nullified by the
attitude assumed by Foreman C. A. Mur
dock, whom they hold responsible for
the lack of effectiveness that has been
the chief distinguishing mark of the
acts of the Grand Jury.
| So grave, indeed, are the differences be
tween the foreman and a majority of the
members of the jury that serious discus
sion has been indulged in as to the ad
visability .of taking steps looking toward
his removal from the position of power
which was given him by Judge Dainger
field when the Jury was impaneled and
1 sworn in. That this has not been done
Is due rather to the fact that the power
j of a majority of the members to depose
i the foreman is questionable,, than to a
lack of desire on the part of those who
; believe tho work of the jury Is being
i hampered and Its effectiveness vitally im-
I paired.
Those members who consent to discuss
i the matter call attention to the investiga
tion of the affairs of the old Board of
I Education that was undertaken by the
'. Grand Jury early in the present year.
(This Investigation has amounted to" noth
ing in the way of practical results, al
though according to views held by some
of the members the opportunities afforded
for a thorough exploitation of the scan
dal were Jinllmlted. At one stage of the
investigation half a score of teachers of
both sexes who were suspected of having
paid fancy prices for their positions in
the department were required to appear
as witnesses before the jury. Foreman
Murdock, acting as inquisitor, would in
terrogate these witnesses about as fol
| lows:
"Did you pay anything for your posi
: tion as teacher In the School Depart-
I ment?" ,
"No; I .-did not."- . âi~ -\y- j
"Very well. That is satisfactory so far
I as I am concerned. You are excused." *.
1 Other members of the jury who sought
to question the witnesses with the object
in view of testing their credibility were
promptly sat upon by the foreman.
Those 'members who now question Fore
man Murdock's fitness for the position
which he occupies point to his record as
a School Director, and cite the fact that
while lie was serving as a member of the
Board of Education he handed in his res
ignation because his associates would not
act according to ins wishes. : bA,b;.'-
During the past two months such little
work as has been performed by the
Grand Jury has had the sanction of but
a bare majority of its members, and on
more than one occasion it was found
necessary to adjourn because no quorum
was present. Members have lost interest
in the work, and there is little prospect
that the future holds better results in
store unless the members are permitted
to choose a. leader whose views are more
in harmony with theirs. '
THE S. P.'S GUESTS.
Speyer and Van Home Back From \
Del Monte.
Jam. D. Speyer, who is out here from
New York reorganizing the Central Pa
cific and at the same time actively en
gaged in promoting harmony among the
occupants of the big building at Mont
gomery and Market streets, returned
yesterday from Hotel del Monte, where,
with his party, he has been a guest since
Thursday of General Hubbard and Ju
lius Kruttschnitt. >
Sir William C. Van Home,, the titled
American president of the Canadian Pa
cific Railroad, has also been a guest of
the railroad people at the Monterey ho
tel, where he and his rather numerous
party have spent the oast few days, ac
companied by J. A. Fillmore. Sir Wil
liam expects to leave soon upon ids re
turn trip, but will first take In the south
ern end of the State.
The date of Mr. Speyer's leave taking
is problematical. Now that he lias elected
his new board of directors for the Cen
tral Pacific, the last details of the gigantic
bond Issue Boon to be announced are being
arranged and it is expected that a meet
ing of the directorate will be called within
a very few days for the purpose of tak
ing definite action upon that phase of the
consolidation scheme.
ACCUSED OF ROBBERY.
James McÃride, 152 Fifth street, began
early yesterday morning to celebrate
Dewey day. Between 2 and 3 o'clock he
dropped into William .Phillips' saloon,
Fifth and Natoma streets, and took part
In a discussion as to the Importance of
the day. To back up his opinion he put
a $5 gold piece on the counter, but no one
accepted his challenge.
Phillips, he alleges, took the gold piece
and put It In his cash register, and when
McÃride demanded its return he was
laughed at. McÃride informed a police
man and Phillips was taken to the City
Prison, where a charge of robbery was
booked against him.
F. R. Hatcher, stepson of M. "Cernltore,
the old gentleman who was robbed of
$150 by Daniel Burke, -a cook, In the Simp
son House, Second and Minna streets,
Sunday night, is determined to push the
charge of robbery against Burke. Only
$115 of the money was recovered and
Burke declines to say what became of
the other $35. The old gentleman is still
suffering from the beating he received
from Burke before being robbed.

Actors' Fete Day.
To-day is the annual holiday of the
Chinese actors, or the celebration of the
birthday of Ton Wing Fong. The old
Chinese legend says that about 1000 years
ago China was very prosperous. Every
body had plenty of money, and in order
that they might enjoy themselves, Tong
Wing Fong invented the theater. Per
formances were given nightly and the
doors were open to everybody. The cele
bration to-day will consist of an all-day
performance in the theater. The doors
will be closed at 5 o'clock and the mem
bers of the troupe will dress themselves
in gala attire and seek such amusements
as suit their individual tastes.

SEAL HERDS ST
POINT REYES
DRIVEN AWAY
Havoc Among the
Rookeries.
THE WORK OF TWO HUNTERS
KILLED, WOUNDED AND SCARED
BY BULLETS.
Ocean Water Reddened by the Blood
of the Fish-Killers, but Their
Bodies Went Out
to Sea.
Death reaped a great harvest at the sea
lion rookeries at Point Reyes yesterday.
The metal-jacketed bullet of the maga
zine rifle has played havoc in the large
herds of the marine animals, and the tide
off the very stormy headland is strewn
with their lifeless carcasses. For some
time past this has been contemplated by
the State Board of Fish and Game Com
missioners, who believed that by thinning
out the herds the fisheries industries of
this locality would be more protected
from the ravages of the beasts. On Fri
day morning Deputy Al Wilson and a
companion started for the rookeries to
slaughter the seals. Upon arriving there
they found that Mr. Wilson's former trip
in February had had some effect. On
that occasion he killed about 150 of the
beasts, and the remainder were somewhat
timid at the report of a rifle.
At any rate, there were fewer than
when he was there before, but still
enough to make the hunting interesting.
It had been supposed that the seals were
in the habit of going to these rookeries
to bring forth their young, but inquiry
leads to the belief that the^Farallones are
the favorite breeding grounds and that
the old sea lions take their young to the
Point Reyes rookeries as soon as they
are able to master the waves. In order to
destroy as many of the fish-killers as
possible it was necessary to look over the
ground before beginning the work of kill
ing. Mr. Wilson recognized at once that
conditions were different from what they
were when he was there before, and two
days were consumed in going over the
rocky cliffs. This is by no means an easy
task. The point is situated on the ex
treme end of the peninsula that projects
out Into the ocean about eight miles from
the line of the mainland. .
The face of the point extends about
three miles northwest and southeast. It
is a wild cliff that, rises out of the ocean
to a height of about 600 feet. At the foot
of this cliff on the northern end the
rookeries are located on ledges and
shelves from ten to twenty feet above
the water. There are but few places
where even the most daring hunter would
venture a descent, for a single misstep
would hurl him down hundreds of feet
upon the jagged rocks over which the
breakers dash Incessantly. As stated, the
first days were devoted to finding a place
where access could be had to these rocks,
near the water, and where : the hunters
could do execution with their firearms.
On . Saturday they found a place where
the animals frequented, and there some
shooting was done and a number were
killed, but not as many as was expected.
it was decided that it would be best to
avoid firing upon the rookeries as long as
possible, because to do so would scatter
the sea lions. At last a desirable loca
tion was found, but to reach it was dan
gerous. Yesterday morning after a
hearty breakfast at the dairy farm of R.
E. Dickson, where the hunters were ac
commodated in fine style, they sallied
forth. After a walk of half an hour they
reached the Government lands on which
the lighthouse and signal station is lo
cated, and began the descent to the wa
ter's edge. A part of the way was down
a rickety ladder, placed there by some
adventurous fisherman, but long out of
use. This is thirty feet long, and below
the rocks are as treacherous as the lad
der, only small shelves projecting to af
ford an unsatisfactory foothold. With
pockets filled with ammunition and rifles
strapped to their backs the men care
fully worked their way down the face of
the cliff, a high gale threatening to dash
them to the depths below every moment.
The descent required fifteen minutes, but
at last a safe footing was obtained near
the water, close to one of the small reefs
around which the seals had to pass on
their way from the Ashing grounds on
the south reef to the rookeries. The hunt
ers crouched behind a small rock to be
sheltered from the wind, but not from the
spray and spume of the sea, which
dashed over them with every roller of the
Pacific.
As the sea lions passed by their appear
ance at the surface was met with a sharp I
report of one or both rifles. Where the
shots were instantly fatal the large
beasts stretched out on the water witnout
a movement, but where the bullets failed
to find the vital spot the commotion was
terrific. The wounded creatures .would
bellow, thrash and flounder â¢ violently,
spouting great streams of blood, for the
animals are very full blooded, and a
wounded sea lion will redden the sea for
yards around. It is safe to say that not
one of the wounded escaped, their very
exertions accomplishing all that the bul
lets failed in. Besides, one of the rifles
shot half metal jacketed soft pointed bul
lets, that expand on coming in contact
with a solid of any kind, and the ex
panded points tore great holes, that
drained the blood very rapidly.
The procession that swam to its death
was varied. Great bulls that weighed
from one ton to a ton and a half, large
cow seals with yellow bodies, . spotted
leopard seals, black barking seals and the
half-grown of all kinds afforded targets
for the hunters.
Many of the killed went to the bottom
at once, and the few that died with air in
their lungs floated out to sea, so tnat
there is but little fear that their bodies
will ever annoy the people along tne
shore. As the run along the cliff de
creased it was decided to make an on
slaught upon the rookeries proper. Gain
ing a point about 150 yards from the rook
eries, for the hunters could get no near
er, a stand was taken upon a point of
rocks in full view of the bellowing, bark
ing beasts. The rifle sights were run un
for the required elevation and a large
quantity of cartridges were placed so as
to be handy for fusillade. There were two
objective, points to be covered. On one
ledge, twelve feet from the water, lay
basking in the sun about seventy-five sea
lions. A little further to the left was a
rough rock upon which rolled and tum
bled many more. Scarcely a foot of the
rocks but were thus covered.
"Are you ready?' asked Wilson. "Turn
loose." The two rifles spoke together.
Then the reports of the two alternated,
the sharp crack of the smokeless powder
arm contrasting strangely with the louder
"bang" of the black powder rifle, It must
have been that the gale was so strong
that the reports were faint to the ears of
the sea lions, for It took them several i
minutes to comprehend that death was
swinging his scythe among them. But
they were soon alarmed by the bellowing
and barking of the wounded and by the
rolling of the dead from the rocks. Then
with. a rush the whole herd tumbled Into
the breakers and disappeared. This gave
an opportunity to replenish the nearly
empty magazines, which, by. the way, had
been emptied and filled once since the
shooting began. Even at that great dis
tance the deadly effect of the bullets was
to be seen. Large patches of blood ap
peared upon the surface of the water,
marking the location of dead or wounded.
The number of these will never be known,
for as stated the swift ocean currents
soon carried away the helpless and de-
timet. â  â¢ ..".-. , â .
No sooner were the mgnzines of the
rifles replenished than the frightened
animals began to appear on the surface
in herds of from three to two dozen and
not a few lots came to the top as thick
as a bunch of bananas, affording the
hunters a splendid chance to get a pot
shot on the lot. Not a few of the stupid
brutes crawled back to the rocks again
only to present fine targets for the de
stroyers. The amount of damage that
can be done by two men with repeaters
is astonishing. When the seals came to
the conclusion that the rocks were no
longer safe they began to swim about,
and all that remained for the hunters to
do was to secure a good place and "plunk
it" to all of the stragglers that got within
range. It is believed - that the breaking
up of the rookeries at Point Reyes will
have the effect of driving the larger part
of the herd to the Farallon Islands.
The shooting of strays will continue to
day and maybe to-morrow, when the
hunters will return to this city.
Lost a Valuable Watch.
John Devenney, 550 Natoma street, was
among the crowd that assembled In Co
lumbia Square yesterday at noon to hear
the gun fired in honor of Admiral Dewey.
He was not there long when he discovered
that his gold watch had been stolen from
his pocket. It had teen neatly slipped
off the chain. He reported his loss at
police headquarters and said he felt it
keenly as the watch was presented to him
by ex-Governor Budd at St. Patrick's
Fair about a year ago for being the most
popular Irishman in the city, and it bore
a suitable inscription.
